His saying: (And for the woman in 'iddah of divorce i.e., maintenance, clothing, and housing are obligatory for the woman in 'iddah of divorce. This is the apparent meaning of the Mukhtasar. Al-Zayla'i mentioned maintenance and housing but did not mention clothing. It is transmitted in al-Dhakhira, al-Khaniyya, al-'Inaya, and al-Mujtaba that the woman in 'iddah is entitled to clothing. They said: Muhammad only did not mention it in the book because the 'iddah is usually not long, so she is independent of it, unless she needs it, then it is prescribed for her. End. So it appears that the clothing of the woman in 'iddah is detailed: if she is independent of it due to the shortness of the period, as when her 'iddah is by menstruation and she menstruates, or by months, then no clothing for her. If she needs it due to the length of the period, as when she has an extended period of purity and does not menstruate, then the judge prescribes it for her. This is what al-Tarsusi refined in Anfa' al-Wasa'il, and it is a good refinement understood from their statements. He made divorce general, so it includes irrevocable and revocable because she is for confinement and she is confined in both regarding a intended ruling, which is the child, since 'iddah is obligatory to preserve the child, so maintenance is obligatory. In al-Mujtaba: Maintenance of 'iddah is like maintenance in marriage, and it falls with the passage of the period except by prescription or settlement. If she borrows against him while he is absent, then if it was by a judge's order, she can claim it back; without a judge's order, there is difference in narrations and shaykhs. End. In al-Dhakhira: Maintenance is obligatory for the woman in 'iddah whether the period is long or short, and her statement regarding its non-expiry is accepted with her oath. If the husband brings evidence of her admission of expiry, he is free from it. If she claims pregnancy, he spends on her between her and two years from the day he divorced her. If she says 'I thought I was pregnant and did not menstruate, and I have an extended period of purity until now, and I think what I have is wind, and I want maintenance until my 'iddah expires,' and the husband says 'you claimed pregnancy and its maximum is two years,' the judge does not pay attention to his statement and maintenance is obligatory on him as long as the 'iddah has not expired, either by three menstruations or by her entering the age of menopause and three months passing after that. If she menstruates during these three months, she starts a new 'iddah by menstruation and maintenance is obligatory for her all that unless a judgment of expiry of 'iddah is made. Similarly in al-Khulasa. An incident occurred in our time where she claimed pregnancy and he did not believe her, so maintenance was prescribed for her on condition that if she is not pregnant she returns it. It is obvious that this condition is invalid. In al-Khulasa: If the woman in 'iddah does not take maintenance until her 'iddah expires, her maintenance falls. This is when it is not prescribed. As for when it is prescribed, al-Sadr al-Qadi mentioned... Maintenance of the absentee from the city, whether there is travel duration between them or not, as in al-Munya. It is appropriate that... of the hidden one in the city and includes the missing person. End. I said: And Fatawa Ahu (al-Sayrafi) ... His saying: (And al-Sarakhsi indicated that it does not fall) In most copies it is thus; in some copies 'it falls' without 'la'. The correct is... So on this, the texts must be corrected. He said: And in al-Nahr: The general statements of the texts indicate what al-Halwani chose. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol. 4 p. 22. 338 Book of Divorce Chapter of Maintenance. The martyr in al-Fatawa al-Sughra from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: In my selected view, it does not fall. End. The dispute is also mentioned in al-Khaniyya. In al-Dhakhira: If the judge ordered her to borrow and she borrowed, she can claim reimbursement from the husband because it is as if he borrowed himself. If the judge did not order her to borrow, then there is dispute. Al-Sarakhsi indicated that it falls where he reasoned: The entitlement to this maintenance is the 'iddah, and the entitled by this cause is like a cause, so the cause must exist for entitlement of claim. Do you not see that if a dhimmi becomes Muslim and has jizya on his head, he is not demanded for any part of it? So here too. This is the correct view. End. So on this, the texts must be corrected because they state that it becomes due by judgment or consent and becomes a debt, but here it does not become a debt by judgment unless the 'iddah has not expired... In al-Dhakhira: The husband is responsible for the housing expense of the woman in 'iddah. If he does not own a house, he rents a house for her and the rent is on him. If he is poor, the woman is ordered to borrow the rent and then claim from him when he becomes wealthy, as is the ruling in maintenance during marriage. If the divorce is irrevocable, if the house is owned by the husband, the husband should leave the house and move away from her, leaving her in that house until her 'iddah expires. Similarly, if the house is rented. If he rents another house for her, it is permissible, but it is better to leave her in the house they were living in before divorce. If the divorce is revocable, al-Khassaf mentioned that he houses her in the house they were living in before divorce, but the husband leaves or moves away from her in a corner of it. End. Also in it: If the woman in 'iddah leaves the house of 'iddah, her maintenance falls as long as she persists in nushuz. If she returns to the husband's house, she has maintenance and housing. Then, leaving the house of 'iddah continuously is not a condition for her maintenance to fall: if she leaves for a time and stays for a time, she does not deserve maintenance. In Fatawa al-Nasafi: The woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce who marries during 'iddah, consummates, and they are separated, and 'iddah is obligatory from both: no maintenance on the second husband due to its invalidity, and she has maintenance on the first if she does not leave the house of 'iddah. If she leaves, then she has none, and she is not described as being nashiz by preventing herself from him here because the divorce is irrevocable and the permission is gone. End. In al-Dhakhira also: If a man settles with his wife regarding her maintenance during 'iddah for a specified dirhams, not increasing it until the 'iddah expires, we see: if her 'iddah is by menstruation, the settlement is not valid due to ignorance; if by months, it is valid due to its absence. If he divorces her or irrevocably separates and then settles with her for housing for dirhams, it is not valid because it leads to nullifying Allah's right regarding housing. In al-Muhit: He divorced her with khul' on condition that she has no maintenance and no housing, so she has housing without maintenance because maintenance is her right, so it is valid to release from it, unlike housing. In al-Walwaljiyya: His nikah... His saying: (The settlement is not valid due to ignorance) In it there is that ignorance of what is settled for does not harm, so ponder. His saying: (Unless it is)

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the maintenance of a woman in 'iddah (waiting period) after divorce. The author first clarifies the ruling: a woman in 'iddah of divorce is entitled to maintenance (nafaqah), clothing (kiswah), and housing (sukna). This is general for both revocable and irrevocable divorce. The reason given is that she is confined for the sake of preserving lineage (al-walad), as the 'iddah is prescribed to determine paternity. The discussion then focuses on clothing: some Hanafi scholars (like Zayla'i) omit it, but majority (Dhakhira, Khaniyya, etc.) consider it obligatory. However, al-Marghinani (author of Hidayah) and others specify that clothing is only due if needed due to a long 'iddah (e.g., extended purity without menstruation); if the 'iddah is short (by menstrual cycles or months), clothing is not required. This is the refined position of Tarsusi. Next, the passage addresses when maintenance falls: it falls with the end of 'iddah unless it was fixed by a judge (farad) or settlement (sulh). If the wife borrowed money without a judge's order, there is disagreement among the mashayikh. The text details the case of a woman claiming pregnancy: the husband must pay maintenance for up to two years from divorce, and her word is accepted regarding non-expiry of 'iddah. An incident is mentioned where a judge prescribed maintenance conditionally—this condition is invalid. The passage then discusses the difference between prescribed and unprescribed maintenance: if prescribed, it does not fall even if 'iddah ends; if not prescribed, it falls. There is a dispute between Sarakhsi (who says it falls) and Halwani (who says it does not) regarding maintenance for an absent husband. The correct view is stated to be that of Sarakhsi—the cause (the 'iddah itself) must exist for the entitlement, analogized to the dhimmi's jizya not being demanded after Islam. The housing expense is the husband's duty; if the house is owned by him, he must vacate and leave it for her during 'iddah (especially in irrevocable divorce). For revocable divorce, he moves away within the same house. Leaving the house of 'iddah for a short time does not cancel maintenance if she returns, but continuous departure cancels it. If she marries during 'iddah and consummates, she has no maintenance from the second husband (invalid marriage), and the first husband's maintenance is conditional on her not leaving the house. Settlement for maintenance during 'iddah is only valid if the 'iddah is by months (known duration), not by menstruation (unknown). Settlement for housing is invalid because housing is a right of Allah (related to the 'iddah itself, not purely financial). In khul', she can waive maintenance but not housing. The passage ends with a comment on the principle that ignorance of the subject matter in settlement (sulh) generally invalidates it, but here ignorance does not harm—so the text requires reflection.

Key Terms
كسوة (kiswah): Clothing that the husband must provide for the woman during 'iddah if needed
عدة بالحيض ('iddah bil-hayd): Waiting period of three menstrual cycles
عدة بالأشهر ('iddah bil-ashhur): Waiting period of three months (for women who do not menstruate)
ممتدة الطهر (mumtaddah al-tuhr): A woman with an extended period of purity (delayed menstruation), resulting in a long 'iddah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Tarsusi's refinement, when does a woman in 'iddah become entitled to clothing (kiswah)?
A) Only if the divorce is revocable
B) Only if she is pregnant
C) Only if her 'iddah is long (e.g., extended purity)
D) Only if the judge orders it without conditions

Key Takeaway
Clothing during 'iddah is wajib only when the 'iddah is long enough that the woman needs it; for short 'iddah (by cycles or months), no clothing is due.

The Falling of Prescribed Maintenance After 'Iddah: The Sarakhsi-Halwani Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the survival of prescribed maintenance after 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Sarakhsi and Halwani regarding whether prescribed maintenance falls when 'iddah ends, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
If maintenance is prescribed by a judge, it becomes a debt and does not fall; if not prescribed, it falls with 'iddah.

A key question in the law of maintenance during 'iddah is what happens if the maintenance was fixed by a judge (farad) or by agreement, and the 'iddah ends. Does the obligation remain as a debt, or is it extinguished? This lesson breaks down the debate between two great Hanafi jurists: Sarakhsi and Halwani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the basic rule
The passage states that maintenance falls with the passage of 'iddah unless it was prescribed (mafrudah) or settled (sulh). This is the baseline: no debt without prescription.
The phrase 'تسقط بمضي المدة إلا بفرض أو صلح' is key.
2
Understand Halwani's position
Halwani (as cited in the text) holds that even if maintenance was prescribed by a judge, it does not fall after 'iddah because it becomes an established debt (dayn) like any other obligation.
His evidence: once a debt is fixed, it is not extinguished by the end of the cause.
3
Understand Sarakhsi's position
Sarakhsi argues that prescribed maintenance also falls after 'iddah because the entitlement to maintenance is directly tied to the cause ('illa) – the 'iddah itself. When the cause ends, the right to claim ends. He uses the analogy of the jizya of a dhimmi who becomes Muslim.
His evidence: 'ألا ترى الذمي إذا أسلم... لم يطالب بشيء منه'.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (Ibn Nujaym) states that the correct view (al-sahih) is that of Sarakhsi: the texts (mutun) need correction to align with this. So the mufta bihi is that prescribed maintenance falls after 'iddah.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the qiyas analogy.

His saying: (And for the woman in 'iddah of divorce i.e., maintenance, clothing, and housing are obligatory for the woman in 'iddah of divorce. This is the apparent meaning of the Mukhtasar. Al-Zayla'i mentioned maintenance and housing but did not mention clothing. It is transmitted in al-Dhakhira, al-Khaniyya, al-'Inaya, and al-Mujtaba that the woman in 'iddah is entitled to clothing. They said: Muhammad only did not mention it in the book because the 'iddah is usually not long, so she is independent of it, unless she needs it, then it is prescribed for her. End. So it appears that the clothing of the woman in 'iddah is detailed: if she is independent of it due to the shortness of the period, as when her 'iddah is by menstruation and she menstruates, or by months, then no clothing for her. If she needs it due to the length of the period, as when she has an extended period of purity and does not menstruate, then the judge prescribes it for her. This is what al-Tarsusi refined in Anfa' al-Wasa'il, and it is a good refinement understood from their statements. He made divorce general, so it includes irrevocable and revocable because she is for confinement and she is confined in both regarding a intended ruling, which is the child, since 'iddah is obligatory to preserve the child, so maintenance is obligatory. In al-Mujtaba: Maintenance of 'iddah is like maintenance in marriage, and it falls with the passage of the period except by prescription or settlement. If she borrows against him while he is absent, then if it was by a judge's order, she can claim it back; without a judge's order, there is difference in narrations and shaykhs. End. In al-Dhakhira: Maintenance is obligatory for the woman in 'iddah whether the period is long or short, and her statement regarding its non-expiry is accepted with her oath. If the husband brings evidence of her admission of expiry, he is free from it. If she claims pregnancy, he spends on her between her and two years from the day he divorced her. If she says 'I thought I was pregnant and did not menstruate, and I have an extended period of purity until now, and I think what I have is wind, and I want maintenance until my 'iddah expires,' and the husband says 'you claimed pregnancy and its maximum is two years,' the judge does not pay attention to his statement and maintenance is obligatory on him as long as the 'iddah has not expired, either by three menstruations or by her entering the age of menopause and three months passing after that. If she menstruates during these three months, she starts a new 'iddah by menstruation and maintenance is obligatory for her all that unless a judgment of expiry of 'iddah is made. Similarly in al-Khulasa. An incident occurred in our time where she claimed pregnancy and he did not believe her, so maintenance was prescribed for her on condition that if she is not pregnant she returns it. It is obvious that this condition is invalid. In al-Khulasa: If the woman in 'iddah does not take maintenance until her 'iddah expires, her maintenance falls. This is when it is not prescribed. As for when it is prescribed, al-Sadr al-Qadi mentioned... Maintenance of the absentee from the city, whether there is travel duration between them or not, as in al-Munya. It is appropriate that... of the hidden one in the city and includes the missing person. End. I said: And Fatawa Ahu (al-Sayrafi) ... His saying: (And al-Sarakhsi indicated that it does not fall) In most copies it is thus; in some copies 'it falls' without 'la'. The correct is... So on this, the texts must be corrected. He said: And in al-Nahr: The general statements of the texts indicate what al-Halwani chose. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol. 4 p. 22. 338 Book of Divorce Chapter of Maintenance. The martyr in al-Fatawa al-Sughra from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: In my selected view, it does not fall. End. The dispute is also mentioned in al-Khaniyya. In al-Dhakhira: If the judge ordered her to borrow and she borrowed, she can claim reimbursement from the husband because it is as if he borrowed himself. If the judge did not order her to borrow, then there is dispute. Al-Sarakhsi indicated that it falls where he reasoned: The entitlement to this maintenance is the 'iddah, and the entitled by this cause is like a cause, so the cause must exist for entitlement of claim. Do you not see that if a dhimmi becomes Muslim and has jizya on his head, he is not demanded for any part of it? So here too. This is the correct view. End. So on this, the texts must be corrected because they state that it becomes due by judgment or consent and becomes a debt, but here it does not become a debt by judgment unless the 'iddah has not expired... In al-Dhakhira: The husband is responsible for the housing expense of the woman in 'iddah. If he does not own a house, he rents a house for her and the rent is on him. If he is poor, the woman is ordered to borrow the rent and then claim from him when he becomes wealthy, as is the ruling in maintenance during marriage. If the divorce is irrevocable, if the house is owned by the husband, the husband should leave the house and move away from her, leaving her in that house until her 'iddah expires. Similarly, if the house is rented. If he rents another house for her, it is permissible, but it is better to leave her in the house they were living in before divorce. If the divorce is revocable, al-Khassaf mentioned that he houses her in the house they were living in before divorce, but the husband leaves or moves away from her in a corner of it. End. Also in it: If the woman in 'iddah leaves the house of 'iddah, her maintenance falls as long as she persists in nushuz. If she returns to the husband's house, she has maintenance and housing. Then, leaving the house of 'iddah continuously is not a condition for her maintenance to fall: if she leaves for a time and stays for a time, she does not deserve maintenance. In Fatawa al-Nasafi: The woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce who marries during 'iddah, consummates, and they are separated, and 'iddah is obligatory from both: no maintenance on the second husband due to its invalidity, and she has maintenance on the first if she does not leave the house of 'iddah. If she leaves, then she has none, and she is not described as being nashiz by preventing herself from him here because the divorce is irrevocable and the permission is gone. End. In al-Dhakhira also: If a man settles with his wife regarding her maintenance during 'iddah for a specified dirhams, not increasing it until the 'iddah expires, we see: if her 'iddah is by menstruation, the settlement is not valid due to ignorance; if by months, it is valid due to its absence. If he divorces her or irrevocably separates and then settles with her for housing for dirhams, it is not valid because it leads to nullifying Allah's right regarding housing. In al-Muhit: He divorced her with khul' on condition that she has no maintenance and no housing, so she has housing without maintenance because maintenance is her right, so it is valid to release from it, unlike housing. In al-Walwaljiyya: His nikah... His saying: (The settlement is not valid due to ignorance) In it there is that ignorance of what is settled for does not harm, so ponder. His saying: (Unless it is)
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the maintenance of a woman in 'iddah (waiting period) after divorce. The author first clarifies the ruling: a woman in 'iddah of divorce is entitled to maintenance (nafaqah), clothing (kiswah), and housing (sukna). This is general for both revocable and irrevocable divorce. The reason given is that she is confined for the sake of preserving lineage (al-walad), as the 'iddah is prescribed to determine paternity. The discussion then focuses on clothing: some Hanafi scholars (like Zayla'i) omit it, but majority (Dhakhira, Khaniyya, etc.) consider it obligatory. However, al-Marghinani (author of Hidayah) and others specify that clothing is only due if needed due to a long 'iddah (e.g., extended purity without menstruation); if the 'iddah is short (by menstrual cycles or months), clothing is not required. This is the refined position of Tarsusi. Next, the passage addresses when maintenance falls: it falls with the end of 'iddah unless it was fixed by a judge (farad) or settlement (sulh). If the wife borrowed money without a judge's order, there is disagreement among the mashayikh. The text details the case of a woman claiming pregnancy: the husband must pay maintenance for up to two years from divorce, and her word is accepted regarding non-expiry of 'iddah. An incident is mentioned where a judge prescribed maintenance conditionally—this condition is invalid. The passage then discusses the difference between prescribed and unprescribed maintenance: if prescribed, it does not fall even if 'iddah ends; if not prescribed, it falls. There is a dispute between Sarakhsi (who says it falls) and Halwani (who says it does not) regarding maintenance for an absent husband. The correct view is stated to be that of Sarakhsi—the cause (the 'iddah itself) must exist for the entitlement, analogized to the dhimmi's jizya not being demanded after Islam. The housing expense is the husband's duty; if the house is owned by him, he must vacate and leave it for her during 'iddah (especially in irrevocable divorce). For revocable divorce, he moves away within the same house. Leaving the house of 'iddah for a short time does not cancel maintenance if she returns, but continuous departure cancels it. If she marries during 'iddah and consummates, she has no maintenance from the second husband (invalid marriage), and the first husband's maintenance is conditional on her not leaving the house. Settlement for maintenance during 'iddah is only valid if the 'iddah is by months (known duration), not by menstruation (unknown). Settlement for housing is invalid because housing is a right of Allah (related to the 'iddah itself, not purely financial). In khul', she can waive maintenance but not housing. The passage ends with a comment on the principle that ignorance of the subject matter in settlement (sulh) generally invalidates it, but here ignorance does not harm—so the text requires reflection.

Key Terms
نفقة مفروضة (nafaqah mafrudah): Maintenance prescribed by a judge or agreed upon by the parties, becoming a specific amount
سقوط (suqut): Falling or extinguishment of a right or obligation
فرض (farad): Prescription (by a judge) of a specific amount of maintenance
سبب (sabab): Cause or effective cause for a ruling; here, the 'iddah is the cause for entitlement to maintenance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the passage, what happens to prescribed (mafrudah) maintenance if the woman in 'iddah does not take it before her 'iddah ends?
A) She can still demand it as a debt
B) It falls and she loses the right to claim it
C) She can demand half of it
D) The judge must decide on a case-by-case basis

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that even prescribed maintenance falls when the 'iddah ends, because the right is contingent on the existence of the 'iddah as a cause.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Does 'Iddah Maintenance Become a Debt After Expiry?
Analysis of whether 'iddah maintenance becomes a debt upon adjudication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi ruling on whether 'iddah maintenance becomes a debt after the 'iddah expires, and explain the qiyas used by Imam al-Sarakhsi to support it.

Core Idea
Maintenance lapses when the 'iddah ends—its cause must persist for the claim to survive.

A woman divorced receives maintenance during her 'iddah. But what if she delays her claim until after the 'iddah ends? Does the unpaid maintenance become a debt she can still demand? Hanafi jurists disagreed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of al-Sarakhsi, who compares it to the lapse of head tax upon conversion, and shows why the relied-upon view is that the claim lapses if not adjudicated during 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central dispute
Two Hanafi positions: al-Halwani says maintenance never lapses; al-Sarakhsi says it lapses if 'iddah expires before adjudication. The commentator sides with al-Sarakhsi.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhhabs.
2
Examine the qiyas (analogy) of al-Sarakhsi
The cause (sabab) of maintenance is the 'iddah itself. Just as the head tax (kharaj) on a dhimmi drops upon embracing Islam because the cause (dhimma) vanishes, maintenance drops when the 'iddah ends.
The analogy hinges on the principle: removal of cause removes the obligation to pay.
3
Understand the condition for debt status
Maintenance becomes a debt only if the 'iddah is still ongoing at the time of judgment. If judgment occurs after 'iddah, it does not create a debt.
This qualifies earlier texts that said it becomes a debt unconditionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب الشهيد في الفتاوى الصغرى عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني أنه قال في المختار عندي أنها لا تسقط اهـ. وذكر الخلاف في الخانية أيضاً. وفي الذخيرة إن كان القاضي أمرها بالاستدانة واستدانت فلها الرجوع على الزوج لأنه كاستدانته بنفسه، وإن لم يأمرها القاضي بالاستدانة ففيه خلاف. وأشار السرخسي إلى أنها تسقط حيث علل فقال : استحقاق هذه النفقة العدة والمستحق بهذا السبب في حكم العلة فلا بد من قيام السبب لاستحقاق المطالبة ألا ترى الذمي إذا أسلم وعليه خراج رأسه لم يطالب بشيء منه فكذا هنا وهو الصحيح اهـ. فعلى هذا لا بد من إصلاح المتون فإنهم صرحوا أنها تجب بالقضاء أو الرضا وتصير ديناً وهنا لا تصير ديناً بالقضاء إلا إذا لم تنقض العدة وهو يرجح المقضي بها تسقط بالطلاق لأنه يشترط للمطالبة بها قيام السبب.
The cause: The martyr in al-Fatawa al-Sughra from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani that he said in al-Mukhtar: In my view it does not lapse. And the disagreement is also mentioned in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Dhakhira: If the judge ordered her to borrow and she borrowed, then she has recourse to the husband because it is like his borrowing himself; but if the judge did not order her to borrow, there is disagreement. And al-Sarakhsi indicated that it lapses when he reasoned: 'The entitlement to this maintenance is for the 'iddah, and that which is entitled by this cause is in the ruling of an effective cause, so the existence of the cause is necessary for entitlement to demand. Do you not see that a dhimmi who becomes Muslim while he has a head tax (kharaj) on him is not demanded for any of it? So also here.' And it is the correct view. Based on this, it is necessary to correct the texts, for they explicitly stated that it is obligatory by judgment or consent and becomes a debt, but here it does not become a debt by judgment unless the 'iddah has not elapsed, and this supports that the adjudicated maintenance lapses with divorce because the existence of the cause is required for the claim.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing whether maintenance for a divorced woman in 'iddah becomes a debt that can be claimed after the 'iddah ends. Al-Halwani holds it does not lapse. Al-Sarakhsi argues it lapses if not adjudicated during 'iddah, using qiyas: just as a dhimmi's head tax is dropped upon embracing Islam, because the cause (dhimma) is gone, so maintenance requires the cause ('iddah residence) to persist. The commentator concludes that the relied-upon position is that maintenance becomes a debt only if the 'iddah is ongoing at the time of judgment; otherwise it lapses. He notes this requires correcting the earlier texts that said it becomes a debt unconditionally.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and housing, during 'iddah.
سبب (sabab): The cause of entitlement, here the 'iddah residence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, if a woman's 'iddah expires before she obtains a court judgment for maintenance, what happens to her claim?
A) She can still claim it as a debt.
B) It lapses because the cause (sabab) is no longer present.
C) It remains but only if she was ordered by the judge to borrow.
D) It becomes a debt on her husband's estate.

Key Takeaway
The enforceability of 'iddah maintenance as a debt depends on whether the cause ('iddah) still exists at the time of adjudication; if the 'iddah has expired, the claim lapses.

Housing Obligation and Distinction Between Ba'in and Raj'i Divorce
Rulings on housing for the 'iddah woman and differences between ba'in and raj'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the housing obligation during 'iddah and distinguish the rules for ba'in and raj'i divorce.

Core Idea
Housing is wajib for the 'iddah woman; in ba'in divorce the husband leaves the house, in raj'i he stays apart.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the husband's duty to provide housing during the 'iddah period. It details the obligations when the husband owns or rents a home and how the living arrangements differ based on whether the divorce is revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on housing
The passage states that housing is wajib upon the husband for the woman in 'iddah. If he does not own a house, he must rent one; if he cannot afford, she may borrow and recoup later.
This mirrors the rule for maintenance during marriage.
2
Distinguish ba'in and raj'i divorce
For ba'in divorce, the husband must leave the house (whether owned or rented) and leave her there. For raj'i divorce, the husband must stay in the same house but separate himself in a corner.
The difference stems from the wife's right to stay in the marital home during the waiting period.
3
Note the option to rent another house
The passage mentions that renting another house for her is permissible, but it is better to leave her in the original home to avoid unnecessary disruption.
This is a recommendation (sunnah) not a requirement.
4
Understand the financial aspect
If the husband is in difficulty, the woman may borrow the rent and later claim it from him when he becomes able. This is analogous to maintenance during marriage.
The husband's inability does not nullify the obligation but defers it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة على الزوج مؤنة سكنى المعتدة، فإن لم يكن له منزل مملوك يكتري منزلاً لها ويكون الكراء عليه، فإن كان معسراً تؤمر المرأة أن تستدين الكراء ثم ترجع على الزوج إذا أيسر كما هو الحكم في النفقة حال قيام النكاح، فإن كان الطلاق بائناً، فإن كان المنزل ملكاً للزوج ينبغي أن يخرج الزوج من المنزل ويعتزل عنها ويتركها في ذلك المنزل إلى انقضاء عدتها، وكذلك إن كان المنزل بالكراء وإن استكرى لها منزلاً آخر يجوز لكن الأفضل أن يتركها في المنزل الذي كانا يسكنان فيه قبل الطلاق، وإن كان الطلاق رجعياً فقد ذكر الخصاف أنه يسكنها في المنزل الذي كانا يسكنان فيه قبل الطلاق لكن الزوج يخرج أو يعتزل عنها في ناحية منه .اهـ. وفيها أيضاً المعتدة إذا خرجت من بيت العدة تسقط نفقتها ما دامت على النشوز، فإن عادت إلى بيت الزوج كان لها النفقة والسكني، ثم الخروج عن بيت العدة على سبيل الدوام ليس بشرط لسقوط نفقتها فإنها إذا خرجت زماناً وسكنت زماناً لا تستحق النفقة وفي فتاوى النسفي : المعتدة عن طلاق بائن إذا تزوجت في العدة ووجد الدخول وفرق بينهما ووجبت العدة منهما لا نفقة على الزوج الثاني لفساد وهي على الأول إذا لم تخرج من بيت العدة، فإن خرجت فلا ولا توصف بالنشوز بمنعها نفسها منه هنا لأن الطلاق بائن والحل زائل .اهـ.
And in al-Dhakhira: Upon the husband is the expense of housing for the woman in 'iddah. If he does not own a house, he rents one for her, and the rent is upon him. If he is in difficulty, the woman is ordered to borrow the rent and then recourse to the husband when he becomes wealthy, as is the ruling for maintenance during marriage. If the divorce is irrevocable (ba'in), then if the house is owned by the husband, he should leave the house and separate from her, leaving her in that house until the end of her 'iddah. Similarly if the house is rented. And if he rents another house for her, it is permissible, but it is better to leave her in the house they were living in before divorce. If the divorce is revocable (raj'i), al-Khassaf mentioned that he provides her housing in the house they were living in before divorce, but the husband leaves or separates from her in a corner of it. And in it also: The woman in 'iddah, if she leaves the house of 'iddah, her maintenance lapses as long as she remains in disobedience (nushuz); if she returns to the marital home, she has maintenance and housing. Then, leaving the house of 'iddah permanently is not a condition for lapse of maintenance; if she leaves for a time and resides for a time, she does not deserve maintenance. And in Fatawa al-Nasafi: The woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce, if she marries during 'iddah and consummation occurs and they are separated, and 'iddah becomes obligatory from both, there is no maintenance on the second husband due to its invalidity, and it is upon the first husband if she does not leave the house of 'iddah; if she leaves, then not, and she is not described as nashiz (disobedient) by preventing herself from him here because the divorce is irrevocable and the connection is severed.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on housing for the 'iddah woman. The husband must provide housing; if he cannot afford, the woman can borrow and claim reimbursement. For ba'in divorce, the husband leaves the house; for raj'i, he stays separate. If the woman leaves the marital home without valid reason, her maintenance lapses even if she returns intermittently. A special case: if a woman in ba'in 'iddah remarries and consummates, she has no maintenance from the second husband, and only from the first if she stays in the marital home.

Key Terms
سكنى (sukna): Housing provided to the 'iddah woman.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage.
رجعي (raj'i): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife during 'iddah without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a revocable (raj'i) divorce, where should the woman stay during 'iddah?
A) She must leave the marital home immediately.
B) She stays in the marital home, and the husband leaves or stays separately in a corner.
C) She must rent separate accommodation at her own expense.
D) She stays in the marital home, and the husband continues to live with her normally.

Key Takeaway
Housing for the 'iddah woman is wajib, with distinct rules for ba'in (husband vacates) and raj'i (husband stays apart).

Effect of the Woman Leaving the 'Iddah House and Special Remarriage Case
Consequences of the woman leaving the 'iddah house and a special case of remarriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how leaving the 'iddah house affects maintenance and apply the ruling in the special case of remarriage during 'iddah.

Core Idea
If the 'iddah woman leaves the marital home without right, her maintenance lapses even if she returns; remarriage during 'iddah has special rules.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, citing al-Dhakhira and Fatawa al-Nasafi, details the consequences of the woman leaving the 'iddah house. It clarifies that even temporary departure affects maintenance, and addresses the unique situation of a woman in ba'in 'iddah who remarries and consummates, leading to no maintenance from the second husband and possible maintenance from the first if she stays.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on leaving the house
The passage states that if the woman leaves the house of 'iddah, her maintenance lapses as long as she remains in nushuz (disobedience). If she returns, maintenance and housing resume.
Leaving without valid cause is considered nushuz.
2
Understand the effect of temporary departure
Leaving permanently is not required; even if she leaves for a time and returns, she does not deserve maintenance during the period of absence.
It is a complete lapse, not proportional.
3
Analyze the special case from Fatawa al-Nasafi
A woman in ba'in 'iddah who marries another man and consummates the marriage has no maintenance from the second husband because the marriage is invalid. She may have maintenance from the first husband if she does not leave the house; if she leaves, she gets nothing.
She is not considered nashiz since the tie with the first husband is severed.
4
Apply the rule to practical scenarios
For example, if a woman leaves to visit family without husband's permission, she loses maintenance. But if she leaves due to abuse or fear, it may be justified and not nushuz.
The rule assumes leaving without valid cause.

Source Text

Key Takeaway
A woman in 'iddah loses maintenance if she leaves the house without cause; but in the special case of remarriage during ba'in 'iddah, she may still have maintenance from the first husband if she stays.

Maintenance for Death 'Iddah and Wife's Disobedience
Rulings on maintenance for death 'iddah, wife's disobedience, and settlement agreements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on maintenance for a widow and a woman separated due to her own sin, and distinguish between the rules for maintenance and housing.

Core Idea
A widow receives no maintenance; a wife separated by her sin forfeits maintenance but retains housing.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies critical distinctions in maintenance obligations during 'iddah. Many assume widows automatically receive support, but Hanafi law holds otherwise. The ruling turns on who caused the separation and whether the wife was disobedient. We will also examine a settlement (sulh) scenario and compare the rulings for maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (sukna).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage covers (1) widow in death 'iddah, (2) separation due to wife's sin (e.g., apostasy), and (3) settlement for maintenance during 'iddah.
Note the distinction between death 'iddah and divorce 'iddah.
2
Extract the proof for no maintenance on widow
Reason: Her 'iddah is an act of worship, not for the husband's right. Additionally, maintenance is owed gradually and the husband has no ownership after death, so it cannot be imposed on heirs.
The text uses 'do you not see that the meaning of establishing purity of womb is not regarded' – meaning the purpose of 'iddah is not solely to determine paternity.
3
Analyze separation due to wife's sin
If separation occurs because of the wife's disobedience (e.g., apostasy, kissing stepson), she is like a nashiz and loses maintenance. But if separation is from her side without sin (e.g., option of puberty), maintenance remains.
The key is whether she confined herself without right.
4
Distinguish maintenance from housing
Even when maintenance drops due to disobedience, housing (sukna) remains wajib. Housing is a right upon her, not a right for her, and does not drop with her sin.
Some scholars (Fath al-Qadir) say housing is due in all cases.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf on the widow
Some early scholars (Ali, Shurayh) allowed spending on the pregnancy from the estate, but the Hanafi position is that it requires judge's permission and is for the pregnancy, not her maintenance.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the widow gets no maintenance, but the executor may spend on the pregnancy if permitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة أيضاً: وإذا صالح الرجل امرأته عن نفقتها ما دامت في العدة على دراهم مسماة لا يزيدها عليها حتى تنقضي العدة ينظر، إن كان عدتها بالحيض لا يجوز الصلح للجهالة، وإن كانت بالأشهر جاز لعدمها، وإذا خلعها أو أبانها ثم صالحها عن السكنى على دراهم لا يجوز لأنه يؤدي إلى إبطال حق الله تعالى في السكنى وفي المحيط : خالعها على أن لا نفقة لها ولا سكنى فلها السكني دون النفقة لأن النفقة حقها فيصح الإبراء عنها دون السكنى. وفي الولوالجية : المختلعة بنفقة عدتها هل تخرج في حوائجها بالنهار تكلموا فيه والمختار أنها لا تخرج لأنها هي التي أبطلت حقها في النفقة فلم يصح الإبطال فيما يؤدي إلى إبطال حق الشرع اهـ.
قوله : (لا الموت (والمعصية أي لا تجب النفقة لمعتدة الموت ولا لمعتدة وقعت الفرقة بينها وبين زوجها بمعصية من جهتها كالردة وتقبيل ابن الزوج، أما المتوفى عنها زوجها فلأن احتباسها ليس لحق الزوج بل لحق الشرع فإن التربص عبادة منها ألا تری أن معنى التعريف عن براءة الرحم ليس بمراعى فيه حتى لا يشترط فيه الحيض فلا تجب نفقتها عليه، ولأن النفقة تجب شيئاً فشيئاً ولا ملك له بعد الموت فلا يمكن إيجابها في ملك الورثة، أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت حاملاً لكنه قال في الظهيرية : وإذا أنفق الوصي على الحامل للحمل فضمنوه يرجع على المرأة بما أنفق إلا أن يكون ذلك بإذن القاضي لأن علياً وشريحاً كانا بريان ذلك للحمل من جميع المال .اهـ. وشمل السكنى والنفقة فلا سكنى لها أيضاً؛ كذا في المبسوط . وأما الفرقة بمعصية من جهتها فلأنها صارت حابسة نفسها بغير حق فصارت كما إذا كانت ناشزة بخلاف المهر بعد الدخول لأنه وجد التسليم في حق المهر بالوطء. قيد بالمعصية أي بمعصيتها لأن الفرقة من قبلها بغير معصية كخيار العتق وخيار البلوغ والتفريق لعدم الكفاءة لا تسقط نفقتها لأنها حبست نفسها بحق كما إذا حبست نفسها لاستيفاء المهر، فالحاصل أن الفرقة إما من قبله أو من قبلها، فإن كانت من قبله فلها النفقة مطلقاً، سواء كانت بمعصية أو بغير معصية، طلاقاً كانت أو فسخاً كطلاقه ولعانه وعنته أو تقبيله بنت زوجته أو إيلائه مع عدم فيئه حتى مضت أربعة أشهر أو إبائه عن الإسلام إذا أسلمت هي أو ارتد هو فعرض عليه الإسلام فلم يسلم. وإن كانت من قبلها، فإن كان بمعصية فلا نفقة لها، وأما السكني فقالوا بوجوبها كما في الخانية وشرح الطحاوي. وفي فتح القدير لها السكنى في جميع الصور لأن القرار في منزل الزوج حق عليها ولا تسقط بمعصيتها، أما النفقة فحق لها فتجازى بسقوطها لمعصيتها. وبما قررناه علم أن المصنف لو قال ولمعتدة الطلاق أو الفسخ إلا إذا وقعت الفرقة في معصيتها فلا نفقة لها إلا السكنى لكان أولى فإن كلامه خال عن معتدة الفسخ والمعصية شاملة لمعصيتها ولمعصيته. وفي الذخيرة: وفرق بين النفقة وبين المهر فإن الفرقة إذا جاءت من قبل المرأة قبل الدخول يسقط المهر، سواء كانت عاصية أو محقة، لأن المهر عوض من كل وجه ولهذا لا يسقط بموت أحدهما فإذا فات العوض بمعنى من جهة من
And in al-Dhakhira also: If a man makes a settlement with his wife for her maintenance as long as she is in 'iddah for a named amount, no increase on her until her 'iddah ends—consider: if her 'iddah is by menstruation, the settlement is invalid due to ignorance (jahalah); if it is by months, it is valid due to its absence. And if he divorces her by khul' or gives her a ba'in divorce, then settles with her for housing for an amount, it is not valid because it leads to nullification of Allah's right regarding housing. And in al-Muhit: If he divorces her by khul' on condition that she has no maintenance or housing, she has housing but not maintenance, because maintenance is her right so remission (ibra') of it is valid, but housing is not. And in al-Walwaljiyya: The woman who initiates khul' (mukhtali'ah) regarding the maintenance of her 'iddah—should she go out for her needs during the day? They discussed it, and the chosen view is that she does not go out, because she herself nullified her right to maintenance, so nullification of what leads to nullification of a right of the Sharia is not valid.

His statement: (Not for death and disobedience): i.e., maintenance is not obligatory for the woman in 'iddah of death, nor for the woman whose separation from her husband occurs due to her own disobedience, such as apostasy or kissing the husband's son. As for the widow, because her confinement is not for the husband's right but for the right of the Sharia; indeed, waiting is an act of worship from her. Do you not see that the meaning of establishing the purity of the womb is not regarded in it, so menstruation is not a condition; hence, maintenance is not obligatory upon him. And because maintenance is owed gradually, and he has no ownership after death, so it cannot be imposed upon the estate of the heirs. He stated it absolutely, so it includes if she is pregnant, but he said in al-Zuhayriyya: If the executor spends on the pregnant woman for the pregnancy and they hold him liable, he recovers from the woman what he spent, unless that was with the permission of the judge, because 'Ali and Shurayh used to deem that for the pregnancy from all the wealth. It includes housing and maintenance, so she has no housing either; as in al-Mabsut. As for separation due to her disobedience, because she becomes one who confines herself without right, so she becomes like a nashiz (disobedient wife), unlike the mahr after consummation because delivery in the case of mahr occurred by intercourse. He restricted it to her disobedience, because separation from her side without disobedience, like the option of emancipation, option of puberty, and separation due to lack of compatibility, does not drop her maintenance, because she confines herself by right, just as if she confined herself to receive the mahr. The conclusion is that separation is either from his side or from her side. If it is from his side, she has maintenance absolutely, whether with or without disobedience, whether divorce or annulment (faskh), like his divorce, li'an, impotence, his kissing his wife's daughter, his ila' without return after four months, or his refusal to embrace Islam when she becomes Muslim or he apostatizes and is presented with Islam but does not accept. If it is from her side, if it is with disobedience, she has no maintenance; as for housing, they said it is obligatory (wajib) as in al-Khaniyya and Sharh al-Tahawi. In Fath al-Qadir, she has housing in all cases, because residing in the husband's house is a right upon her and does not fall by her disobedience; as for maintenance, it is a right for her, so she is punished by its loss due to her disobedience. Based on what we have established, it is known that if the author had said: 'And for the woman in 'iddah of divorce or annulment, unless the separation occurs due to her disobedience, then no maintenance for her, only housing,' it would have been better, for his speech excludes the woman in 'iddah of annulment, and disobedience includes her disobedience and his. And in al-Dhakhira: A distinction is made between maintenance and mahr: if separation occurs from the woman's side before consummation, the mahr drops whether she is disobedient or rightful, because mahr is compensation from every aspect, and for this reason it does not drop with the death of either; so when the compensation is lost from one side...
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings. First, settlement for maintenance during 'iddah: valid only if the 'iddah is by months (known duration), not by menstruation (unknown). Settlement for housing is invalid. The mukhalla'ah (khul' initiator) may not go out for needs, as she forfeited maintenance. Commenting on the matn (Kanz), the author explains that no maintenance is due for 'iddah of death or when separation is due to the wife's sin (like apostasy). For death 'iddah, it is an act of worship; maintenance cannot be imposed on heirs. For disobedience, the wife is like a nashiz. However, if separation is from the wife's side without sin (e.g., option of puberty), maintenance remains. The critical distinction: if separation is from the husband's side, maintenance is always due; if from the wife's side, maintenance drops only if she is sinful. Housing, however, is always due according to some authorities. The text ends with a comparison to mahr, which drops if the wife initiates separation before consummation regardless of sin.

Key Terms
معتدة الموت (mu'taddat al-mawt): Woman observing 'iddah due to husband's death.
صلح (sulh): Settlement between husband and wife regarding 'iddah maintenance.
مختلعة (mukhtali'ah): Woman who initiates divorce by khul' (redemption).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a widow not entitled to maintenance during her 'iddah according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because she inherits from her husband instead.
B) Because her 'iddah is an act of worship, not for the husband's right, and maintenance cannot be imposed on heirs.
C) Because she is considered a nashiz (disobedient).
D) Because the husband's death automatically terminates all obligations.

Key Takeaway
A widow receives no maintenance; a wife separated due to her own sin loses maintenance but retains housing.

Ruling on a Mukhalla'ah Going Out During 'Iddah
Ruling on whether a mukhalla'ah may go out during her 'iddah for needs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a mukhalla'ah may leave the house during her 'iddah and explain the legal reasoning based on the invalidation of Shari'ah rights.

Core Idea
The mukhalla'ah may not leave the house during 'iddah because her forfeiture of nafaqah does not override the Shari'ah right of 'iddah residence.

In this lesson, we examine the specific ruling for a mukhalla'ah during her 'iddah. While she forfeits her right to nafaqah through khul', the obligation to remain in the marital home during 'iddah is a Shari'ah right that cannot be invalidated by her own action. This lesson clarifies the chosen Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act in question is a mukhalla'ah (woman who obtained khul') going out during her 'iddah period for daytime needs. The ruling is that she may not go out at all.
2
Understand the reasoning: invalidation of Shari'ah right
The mukhalla'ah forfeited her right to nafaqah when she agreed to khul'. However, this forfeiture cannot be extended to invalidate the Shari'ah right of 'iddah residence, which is a separate obligation imposed by the Lawgiver.
The key distinction is between financial rights (nafaqah) and physical obligations (residence).
3
Distinguish from other types of divorce
In a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), the wife retains nafaqah and must also stay in the home. The mukhalla'ah differs because she forfeits nafaqah but the residence obligation remains, emphasizing that the residence right is independent of financial support.
4
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a woman who obtains khul' and needs to go out for work or errands. According to this ruling, she may not leave the house for non-essential purposes. If there is a genuine necessity, a different ruling may apply, but the default is prohibition.
Modern applications often require distinguishing between essential and non-essential outings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المختلعة بنفقة عدتها هل تخرج في حوائجها بالنهار تكلموا فيه والمختار أنها لا تخرج لأنها هي التي أبطلت حقها في النفقة فلم يصح الإبطال فيما يؤدي إلى إبطال حق الشرع اهـ.
Regarding the mukhalla'ah, while she is entitled to nafaqah during her 'iddah, is she permitted to go out for her needs during the day? They discussed this, and the chosen opinion is that she may not go out, because she is the one who invalidated her right to nafaqah, and invalidation is not valid in what leads to invalidating the right of the Shari'ah.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the mukhalla'ah (a woman who obtains khul') and her going out during the 'iddah period. The issue is whether she may go out during the day to fulfill her needs. The chosen (mukhtar) opinion is that she may not go out. The reasoning is that she herself invalidated her right to nafaqah through khul', and this invalidation cannot be extended to the point of invalidating a Shari'ah right, which includes the obligation of staying in the marital home during 'iddah. The passage does not provide an external proof; it is a statement of the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
المختلعة (al-mukhalla'ah): A woman who obtains a khul' divorce, i.e., she pays compensation to her husband to dissolve the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding a mukhalla'ah going out during her 'iddah for daytime needs?
A) She may go out freely.
B) She may go out only with the husband's permission.
C) She may not go out at all.
D) She may go out but must return before night.

Key Takeaway
She forfeits nafaqah but not the Shari'ah right of 'iddah residence; thus she may not leave the home.

Non-Obligation of Nafaqah for 'Iddah of Death and Sin-Based Separation
Ruling with proof on nafaqah for two 'iddah categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah for a widow and a woman separated due to her own sin, and explain the proofs and exceptions.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is not due for widow's 'iddah or sin-based separation; housing may still be due.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two categories where nafaqah is not obligatory during 'iddah: the widow and the woman whose separation is caused by her own sin (like apostasy or intimate touch of a mahram). The reasoning differs: the widow's 'iddah is an act of worship for Allah, not for the husband's right, while the sinful woman's confinement is without right, like a nashizah. Understanding these rulings and their proofs is essential for distinguishing between nafaqah and housing, and between 'iddah of death and divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories and their ruling
The passage states that nafaqah is not obligatory for (1) a woman observing 'iddah due to her husband's death, and (2) a woman whose separation occurred due to her own sin (e.g., apostasy, kissing a stepson).
The ruling applies regardless of pregnancy.
2
Examine the proof for the widow
The proof is that her 'iddah is an act of worship (ta'abbud) for the right of Shari'ah, not for the husband's right. Also, nafaqah is owed progressively, and the husband has no ownership after death, so it cannot be imposed on the heirs.
The practice of 'Ali and Shuraih is cited for pregnancy spending from the estate.
3
Examine the proof for sin-based separation
The wife confined herself without right, similar to a nashizah. However, if separation is from her side without sin (e.g., option of puberty), nafaqah is due because she confined herself by right.
Distinguish from mahr: mahr drops if separation from wife before consummation even without sin.
4
Distinguish nafaqah from housing (sukna)
While nafaqah is not due, housing remains obligatory for the sinful woman according to most Hanafi scholars, as staying in the husband's home is a duty that does not drop due to sin.
For the widow, housing is also not due according to the passage, though other sources may differ.
5
Apply the general principle for separation
If separation originates from the husband, nafaqah is due absolutely. If from the wife and sinful, nafaqah drops; if from the wife without sin, nafaqah remains.
This principle covers talaq, faskh, li'an, 'innah, etc.

Source Text

قوله : (لا الموت (والمعصية أي لا تجب النفقة لمعتدة الموت ولا لمعتدة وقعت الفرقة بينها وبين زوجها بمعصية من جهتها كالردة وتقبيل ابن الزوج، أما المتوفى عنها زوجها فلأن احتباسها ليس لحق الزوج بل لحق الشرع فإن التربص عبادة منها ألا تری أن معنى التعريف عن براءة الرحم ليس بمراعى فيه حتى لا يشترط فيه الحيض فلا تجب نفقتها عليه، ولأن النفقة تجب شيئاً فشيئاً ولا ملك له بعد الموت فلا يمكن إيجابها في ملك الورثة، أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت حاملاً لكنه قال في الظهيرية : وإذا أنفق الوصي على الحامل للحمل فضمنوه يرجع على المرأة بما أنفق إلا أن يكون ذلك بإذن القاضي لأن علياً وشريحاً كانا بريان ذلك للحمل من جميع المهر بالوطء. قيد بالمعصية أي بمعصيتها لأن الفرقة من قبلها بغير معصية كخيار العتق وخيار البلوغ والتفريق لعدم الكفاءة لا تسقط نفقتها لأنها حبست نفسها بحق كما إذا حبست نفسها لاستيفاء المهر، فالحاصل أن الفرقة إما من قبله أو من قبلها، فإن كانت من قبله فلها النفقة مطلقاً، سواء كانت بمعصية أو بغير معصية، طلاقاً كانت أو فسخاً كطلاقه ولعانه وعنته أو تقبيله بنت زوجته أو إيلائه مع عدم فيئه حتى مضت أربعة أشهر أو إبائه عن الإسلام إذا أسلمت هي أو ارتد هو فعرض عليه الإسلام فلم يسلم. وإن كانت من قبلها، فإن كان بمعصية فلا نفقة لها، وأما السكني فقالوا بوجوبها كما في الخانية وشرح الطحاوي. وفي فتح القدير لها السكنى في جميع الصور لأن القرار في منزل الزوج حق عليها ولا تسقط بمعصيتها، أما النفقة فحق لها فتجازى بسقوطها لمعصيتها. وبما قررناه علم أن المصنف لو قال ولمعتدة الطلاق أو الفسخ إلا إذا وقعت الفرقة في معصيتها فلا نفقة لها إلا السكنى لكان أولى فإن كلامه خال عن معتدة الفسخ والمعصية شاملة لمعصيتها ولمعصيته. وفي الذخيرة: وفرق بين النفقة وبين المهر فإن الفرقة إذا جاءت من قبل المرأة قبل الدخول يسقط His statement: '(not death and disobedience)' means nafaqah is not obligatory for a woman observing 'iddah due to death, nor for a woman whose separation from her husband occurred due to a sin on her part, such as apostasy or kissing the stepson. As for the widow, this is because her confinement (in 'iddah) is not for the husband's right but for the right of the Shari'ah; indeed, the waiting period is an act of worship from her. Do you not see that the meaning of [waiting for] clearing the womb is not considered therein, so that menstruation is not a condition, thus nafaqah is not obligatory upon him? And because nafaqah is owed bit by bit, and he has no ownership after death, so it cannot be made obligatory upon the heirs' property. He made it absolute, so it includes the case where she is pregnant, but he said in al-Zahiriyyah: If the executor spends on the pregnant woman for the pregnancy and they make him liable, he can recover from the woman what he spent, unless that was with the qadi's permission, because 'Ali and Shuraih used to rule that it is for the pregnancy from the entire estate. End quote. It includes both housing and nafaqah, so she has no housing either; thus in al-Mabsut. As for separation due to her sin, it is because she confined herself without right, so she becomes like a nashizah. This is unlike the mahr after consummation, because delivery occurred in the case of mahr through intercourse. He restricted it to sin, i.e., her sin, because separation from her side without sin, such as option of emancipation, option of puberty, and separation due to lack of kafa'ah, does not drop her nafaqah, because she confined herself by right, just as when she confines herself to collect the mahr. The conclusion is that separation is either from him or from her. If it is from him, she has nafaqah absolutely, whether with sin or without sin, whether it is by talaq or faskh, such as his talaq, li'an, 'innah, kissing his stepdaughter, or ila' without returning after four months, or his refusal to accept Islam when she becomes Muslim or he apostatizes and is offered Islam and does not accept. If it is from her side, then if it is with sin, she has no nafaqah. As for housing, they said it is obligatory, as in al-Khaniyyah and Sharh al-Tahawi. In Fath al-Qadir: She has housing in all cases, because staying in the husband's home is a duty upon her and does not drop due to her sin. As for nafaqah, it is her right, so she is requited by its dropping due to her sin. By what we have established, it is known that if the author had said 'and for a woman of talaq or faskh, except when separation occurs due to her sin, then she has no nafaqah but has housing,' it would have been better, because his statement lacks mention of the woman of faskh, and 'sin' includes her sin and his sin. In al-Dhakhirah: There is a difference between nafaqah and mahr. If separation comes from the woman before consummation, the mahr drops, whether she is sinful or rightful, because mahr is compensation in every respect, and therefore it does not drop due to death of either. So when the compensation is lost from the side of the one who owns the compensation, it drops. As for nafaqah, it is compensation in one respect and a gift in another respect. So when it is between them [as compensation], it is considered compensation when it comes due to a sinful cause, and a gift when it comes due to a rightful cause. His statement: (When it comes from the woman before consummation) – al-Ramli said: He restricted it to before consummation because after consummation it does not drop in any case, due to the safety of the compensation through consummation, as they explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof regarding the non-obligation of nafaqah for two categories of women in 'iddah: (1) the widow (mutawaffa 'anha zawjuha) and (2) a woman whose separation from her husband occurred due to her own sin (ma'siyah), such as apostasy or kissing the stepson. The commentator first addresses the widow. The proof is that her 'iddah is an act of worship (ta'abbud) for the right of Allah, not for the husband's right. Unlike the 'iddah of divorce, which aims to clear the womb, the widow's 'iddah does not require menstruation to be considered; hence it is purely devotional. Since nafaqah is owed progressively (shay'an fa shay'an) and the husband has no ownership after death, it cannot be imposed on the heirs' property. Even if she is pregnant, the general ruling applies, but there is a special case from al-Zahiriyyah: if the executor spends on the pregnancy, he can recover from the woman unless the qadi permitted it, based on the practice of 'Ali and Shuraih. The commentator notes that housing (sukna) is also not due for the widow. For separation due to the wife's sin, the reasoning is that she confined herself (habasat nafsaha) without right, like a nashizah (rebellious wife). However, if the separation is from her side but without sin (e.g., exercising an option or due to lack of kafa'ah), her nafaqah does not drop because she confined herself by right, akin to a wife detaining herself to collect her mahr. The passage then explains a general principle: if separation originates from the husband, nafaqah is due absolutely, whether or not there is sin, and whether it is talaq or faskh. If from the wife, and it is sinful, nafaqah drops; but most scholars hold that housing (sukna) remains obligatory because it is a duty (haqq) upon her and does not drop due to sin. The commentator suggests the author's wording could be improved. Finally, a quote from al-Dhakhirah distinguishes nafaqah from mahr: mahr drops if separation from wife before consummation regardless of sin because it is pure compensation; nafaqah is part compensation and part gift, so it drops only when the separation is due to the wife's sin, but not when it is rightful.

Key Terms
معتدة الموت (mu'taddat al-mawt): A woman observing 'iddah due to her husband's death.
ناشزة (nashizah): A wife who is disobedient and refuses to fulfill her marital duties without legal justification.
سكنى (sukna): The right to housing during 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because the Quran explicitly exempts her.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah is not due for 'iddah of death because it is an act of worship, and for sin-based separation because the wife confined herself without right; housing, however, remains due for the sinful woman.

The True Cause of Nafaqah Dropping After Bain Talaq
Expansion of the ruling on nafaqah after bain talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why apostasy drops nafaqah but allowing the son does not, and identify the underlying principle of confinement in the 'iddah home.

Core Idea
Nafaqah after bain talaq depends on actual confinement in the 'iddah home, not on the wife's sin.

At first glance, it seems contradictory: apostasy after bain talaq drops nafaqah, but allowing the husband's son (a grave sin) does not. This passage from Bahr al-Raiq unravels the true legal cause (illah) behind these rulings. The key is not the sin itself, but whether the woman is physically confined in the 'iddah home. Let's trace the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the two surface rulings
Identify the ruling for apostasy (nafaqah drops) and for allowing the son (nafaqah continues).
Both occur after bain talaq.
2
Identify the surface distinction
The apostate is imprisoned by the state until repentance; the woman who allows the son is not imprisoned. The commentator says that is why nafaqah drops in the first case but not the second.
Imprisonment prevents 'iddah confinement.
3
Uncover the deeper principle
The underlying cause (illah) is actual confinement in the 'iddah home. If the apostate were not imprisoned, she would still get nafaqah. Similarly, if the woman who allows the son does not confine herself, she loses nafaqah.
Apostasy and sin are not independent causes.
4
Apply the principle to edge cases
If the apostate repents and returns to confinement, nafaqah revives. If she goes to dar al-harb, 'iddah ends and nafaqah does not return. Compare to the nashizah who returns to obedience.
The principle of confinement is consistent.
5
Distinguish from raj'i talaq
In raj'i talaq, if the wife commits a sin like allowing the son, nafaqah drops because the separation is due to her sin, not because of confinement. The passage restricts the discussion to bain talaq.
In raj'i, the marriage is not fully severed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وردتها بعد البت تسقط نفقتها لا تمكين ابنه يعني لو طلقها بائناً ثم ارتدت سقطت نفقتها، ولو مكنت ابن زوجها بعد البينونة لا تسقط مع أن الفرقة فيهما بالطلاق لا من جهتها بمعصية لأن المرتدة تحبس حتى تتوب ولا نفقة للمحبوسة والممكنة لا تحبس فلهذا تقع الفرقة وفي الحقيقة لا فرق بين المسئلتين لأن المرتدة بعد البينونة لو لم تحبس تجب لها النفقة كما في غاية البيان والمحيط كالممكنة والممكنة إذا لم تلزم بيت العدة لا نفقة لها، فليس للردة أو التمكين دخل في الإسقاط وعدمه، بل إن وجد الاحتباس في بيت العدة وجبت وإلا فلا. ولو حبست المعتدة للردة ثم تابت ورجعت تجب النفقة لعود الاحتباس كالناشزة إذا عادت لزوال المانع بخلاف المبانة بالردة إذا أسلمت لا تعود نفقتها لسقوط نفقتها أصلاً بمعصيتها والساقط لا يعود ولو لحقت بدار الحرب ثم عادت وتابت فلا نفقة لها لسقوط العدة بالالتحاق حكماً لتباين الدارين لأنه بمنزلة الموت فانعدم السبب الموجب، قيد بالطلاق البائن لأن المعتدة عن رجعي إذا طاوعت ابن زوجها أو قبلها بشهوة فلا نفقة لها لأن الفرقة لم تقع بالطلاق وإنما وقعت بسبب وجد منها وهو معصيتها. وأطلقه فشمل البائن بالواحدة أو بالثلاث وما في الهداية من تقييده بالثلاث اتفاقي وفي المحيط : الأصل أن كل امرأة لا نفقة لها يوم الطلاق فليس لها النفقة أبداً إلا الناشزة كالمعتدة عن النكاح الفاسد والأمة المزوجة إذا لم يبوئها المولى بيتاً .اهـ. ثم قال بعده ولو طلقها وهي مبوأة فلها النفقة، فإن أخرجها المولى بطلت فإن أعادها عادت النفقة فلو بوأها بعد الطلاق الرجعي وجبت النفقة لأنها منكوحة بخلاف المبانة .
His statement: 'and her apostasy after bain (irrevocable) talaq drops her nafaqah, but allowing his son does not drop it' means: if he divorces her irrevocably (ba'in) and then she apostatizes, her nafaqah drops; but if she allows her husband's son (access) after the irrevocable divorce, it does not drop, even though in both cases the separation is by talaq, not from her side by sin. This is because the apostate is imprisoned until she repents, and there is no nafaqah for the imprisoned one, while the woman who allows (the son) is not imprisoned. Hence the difference. In reality, there is no difference between the two issues because if the apostate after bain were not imprisoned, nafaqah would be due to her, as in Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Muhit, similar to the woman who allows; and the woman who allows, if she does not confine herself to the 'iddah home, has no nafaqah. So apostasy or allowing has no role in the dropping or not; rather, if confinement in the 'iddah home is found, nafaqah is due, otherwise not. If the 'iddah-observing woman is imprisoned for apostasy and then repents and returns, nafaqah becomes due because confinement returns, like the nashizah when she returns, due to removal of the impediment. This is unlike the woman separated by apostasy (mubannah) when she becomes Muslim: her nafaqah does not return because it dropped already due to her sin, and what has dropped does not return. Even if she goes to dar al-harb and then returns and repents, she has no nafaqah because the 'iddah is deemed to have ended due to the difference of the two abodes, as it is like death, so the cause (of nafaqah) is absent. He restricted it to bain talaq because the 'iddah-observing woman of raj'i talaq, if she acquiesces to the husband's son or kisses him with desire, has no nafaqah, because the separation did not occur by talaq but by something from her, i.e., her sin. And he made it absolute, so it includes bain by one or three divorces. What is in al-Hidayah restricting it to three is coincidental. In al-Muhit: The principle is that every woman who has no nafaqah on the day of talaq never gets nafaqah again, except the nashizah, like the 'iddah-observing woman from an invalid marriage and the married slave woman if the master has not provided her a home. End quote. Then he said after it: If he divorces her while she is provided a home (mubawwa'ah), she has nafaqah; if the master removes her, it is void; if he returns her, nafaqah returns. If he provides her a home after raj'i talaq, nafaqah becomes due because she is a wife, unlike the mubannah.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding two specific cases after a bain (irrevocable) talaq: (1) if the wife apostatizes, her nafaqah drops; (2) if she allows the husband's son (from another wife) to have access to her (e.g., intimate), her nafaqah does not drop. The commentator explains the reasoning: the apostate is imprisoned (for apostasy) and thus not confined for 'iddah; the one who allows is not imprisoned, so her nafaqah continues. However, in reality, the determining factor is actual confinement in the 'iddah home: if she is physically present in the home, nafaqah is due; otherwise not. Apostasy and allowing the son are just indicators, not independent causes. If the apostate repents and returns to confinement, nafaqah revives, similar to a nashizah who returns to obedience. But if the apostate ends her 'iddah by going to dar al-harb, the 'iddah is considered complete, so no nafaqah returns. The passage then clarifies that this applies only to bain talaq, not raj'i, because in raj'i, if she commits a sin like kissing the son, it drops nafaqah since the separation occurs due to her sin. It also cites a principle from al-Muhit: a woman who has no nafaqah on the day of talaq never gets it again, except the nashizah, and gives examples like 'iddah from an invalid marriage or a slave woman not provided a home.

Key Terms
البائن (al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce, after which the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage and mahr.
تمكين ابنه (tamkin ibnihi): Allowing the husband's son (from another wife) to have sexual access or forbidden intimacy.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Non-Muslim territory where the Muslim law does not apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does apostasy after bain talaq drop nafaqah according to the passage?
A) Because she loses her right to 'iddah by leaving Islam.
B) Because she is imprisoned for apostasy and not confined for 'iddah.
C) Because the marriage is automatically annulled.
D) Because she is no longer a wife.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah after bain talaq hinges on actual confinement in the 'iddah home, not on the wife's sin or apostasy.
