Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Freedom with Ambiguous Condition in Manumission
Analyzes a case where the condition specifies both outcomes and the dispute about judging slavery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the daughter is fully free according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and evaluate Imam Muhammad's opposing view based on the principle of certainty.

Core Idea
When the condition covers both outcomes, the daughter is definitely free; doubt only affects the mother's half-freedom.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a nuanced case of conditional manumission where the master's condition covers both possible first births: a male frees the mother, a female frees the daughter. When twins are born and the order is unknown, the matn rules that the daughter is free in all circumstances, the mother half-free, and the son a slave. However, a rare transmission from Imam Muhammad in al-Kaysaniyat challenges this, arguing that certainty is required. Let's explore the reasoning behind each position and the preferred Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
Parse the condition formulation
Identify that the condition is two-sided: 'If first is male, mother free; if first is female, daughter free.' This means one of the two conditions must occur.
Contrast with a condition on only one side (e.g., 'if male, you are free'), where doubt is possible.
2
Trace the main ruling's logic
Follow the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: If daughter is born first, she is freed by condition; if son is born first, mother is freed and daughter is born free as a dependent. Hence daughter is always free. Mother is free only when son is first, so half-free. Son is never freed because condition does not apply to him.
This is the ruling in the matn and in al-Jami' al-Saghir.
3
Analyze Muhammad's doubt argument
Imam Muhammad argues that since the order is unknown, we cannot be certain that the condition for freedom (e.g., daughter being first) occurred. Certainty is required for manumission, so no one is freed. The master must swear and all remain slaves.
This view is recorded in al-Kaysaniyat, a Nawadir work.
4
Resolve the dispute with Hanafi principle
The preferred Hanafi position is that when the condition covers both existence and non-existence, one outcome must have occurred, eliminating doubt about the daughter. The mother's half-freedom is based on probability. Al-Nihayah's criticism of Muhammad's view is noted, and the Zahir al-Riwayah position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is adopted for fatwa.
The tarjih by later scholars supports the matn's ruling.

His statement: (And if he said, 'If the first child you give birth to is a male, you are free' and she gave birth to a male or a female and it was not known which was first, then the male is a slave and half of the mother and the female are free) because each one of them is freed in one state and not another: that is, if she gave birth to the boy first, the mother is freed by the condition and the girl because she follows her since the mother was free when she gave birth to her, and she is enslaved in another state, which is when she gave birth to the girl first due to the absence of the condition, so half of each is freed and she works to pay for the half. As for the boy, he is enslaved in both states, so he is a slave. And this answer is as you see. His statement: (It should not be a clarification, etc.) Some virtuous scholars said there is brevity, and the detail is that if the ambiguous divorce is revocable, then divorcing a specific woman is not clarification, whether it is revocable or irrevocable. And if it is irrevocable, then... In al-Jami' al-Saghir, there is no disagreement regarding it. And what is mentioned from Muhammad in al-Kaysaniyat regarding this issue is that he does not rule for the freedom of any of them because we are not certain about freedom, and considering states after certainty of freedom is not permissible, and it is not permissible to effect freedom based on doubt. For this reason, al-Tahawi ruled that Muhammad was initially with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, then he retracted. And in al-Nihayah from al-Mabsut, that this answer is not the answer for this chapter. Rather, in this chapter, no freedom is ruled for any, but the master is made to swear by Allah that he does not know that she gave birth to the girl first. If he refuses, his refusal is like his acknowledgment, and if he swears, then all are slaves. As for the answer for this chapter, it is only in the case where he said, 'If the first child you give birth to is a boy, you are free, and if it is a girl, she is free' and she gave birth to both and he does not know which is first, then the boy is a slave, the girl is free, and half of the mother is freed. There is no doubt that this is not the answer of the book because in this scenario, the entire girl is freed in all circumstances because if she gave birth to the girl first, she is freed by the condition, and if she gave birth to the boy first, she is freed as a dependant of the mother. As for half freedom of the mother, it is because she is freed when the boy is born first and remains a slave when the girl is born first. And the answer of the book is that half of her is freed along with half of the mother. And in al-Nihayah, what is in al-Kaysaniyat is adopted because when the condition whose existence is not certain is on one side, the statement of the one who denies its existence is accepted, as if he said, 'If you enter the house tomorrow, you are free' and tomorrow passes and he does not know if he entered the house or not, due to doubt about the condition of freedom. Similarly, doubt arises about the condition of freedom, which is the birth of the boy first. However, if the condition is mentioned on both sides of existence and non-existence, then one of them necessarily exists, and in that case consideration of states is needed. If you say: In the issue of the book, it is assumed they agree on not knowing which came first, so why does he swear (the master) when there is no claim or dispute? I say: It is interpreted as a claim by an outsider for the sake of God concerning the freedom of the mother or her daughter due to the occurrence of the condition. And it is known that if the mother denies freedom and it is attested to, it is accepted. So accordingly, it is permissible for a man to claim for the sake of God if there is no evidence, so that the master swears in the hope that he will refuse. This, but what is mentioned in al-Mabsut in its reasoning explicitly states that the mother claims freedom and the master denies, and the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath. This indicates that this is in the case of the mother's claim, which is different from the scenario in the book. And know that what is mentioned in al-Nihayah regarding preferring what is in al-Kaysaniyat essentially nullifies the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, even though no rare transmission has been reported from them contradicting that in the answer. And his reasoning that the condition that is on one side... may be considered in that it is for apparent conditions, not hidden ones. Hence al-Mabsut restricted it where he said: If he said, 'If you do such-and-such, you are free' and it is among apparent matters like fasting, prayer, or entering a house, and the slave says 'I did it', he is not believed except with evidence, contrary to if he said 'If you love me'... Divorce of a specific woman is revocable, so likewise. And if it is irrevocable, it is clarification because it is known that the irrevocable does not affect the irrevocable. His statement: (What he knows, whether she gave birth to a girl or not, is as in most copies, and thus I saw it in al-Fath, and in some copies it is corrected by replacing 'girl' with 'boy' and it is apparent.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with a more complex scenario of ambiguous manumission where the condition is formulated to cover both possibilities: 'If the first child is a male, you (the mother) are free; if it is a female, she (the daughter) is free.' The mother gives birth to twins, a boy and a girl, and the order is unknown. The matn gives a ruling: the male is a slave, half of the mother is free, and half of the female is free. However, the commentary reveals a dispute. A different ruling is attributed to Imam Muhammad in al-Kaysaniyat: he would not rule for the freedom of any, because certainty is required for manumission. This position seems to contradict the matn. Al-Tahawi thought Muhammad initially agreed with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (who presumably followed the matn) and later retracted. Al-Nihayah reports from al-Mabsut that the correct answer in this chapter is that the master is made to swear, and if he swears, all remain slaves. The passage then clarifies that the matn's answer (half freedom) applies to a different formulation: when the condition is 'if a boy, you are free; if a girl, she is free'. In that formulation, the girl is always free (either by condition or by following the mother), the boy is a slave, and the mother is half-free. But in the matn's scenario as usually understood, the condition is only on one side ('if male, you are free'), so the situation is different. The passage ends with a textual note about variant readings.

Key Terms
الكيسانيات (al-Kaysaniyat): A collection of rulings attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, possibly containing rare or later views.
الشرط في طرف واحد (al-shart fi taraf wahid): A condition that only specifies one outcome, e.g., 'if X happens, then Y is free' with no alternative stated.
الحسبة (al-hisbah): A legal action taken by a third party for the sake of God, without personal interest, to uphold rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where the master says, 'If the first child is a boy, you (mother) are free; if it is a girl, she (daughter) is free,' and the order is unknown, what is the status of the daughter according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?
A) She is a slave because the condition is not certain.
B) She is free only if she was born first.
C) She is free in all circumstances.
D) She is half-free.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi position is that the daughter is free in all circumstances, the mother half-free, and the son a slave, as per the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Scope of 'Mamluk' in Emancipation Oaths: The Fetus
Defining the scope of 'mamluk' in emancipation oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a fetus is not independently a 'mamluk' in oaths of emancipation and distinguish the four scenarios given in the text.

Core Idea
A fetus is owned dependently, not independently, so it is not included in the word 'mamluk' unless the mother is freed with it.

When someone makes an oath to free their slaves, it matters what exactly counts as a 'slave'. Does an unborn fetus count? The Hanafi school clarifies that the term 'mamluk' (slave) in such oaths refers to an independent human being, not a fetus that is dependently owned as part of the mother. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the four distinct scenarios that illustrate when the fetus is or is not freed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the core legal statement: the word 'mamluk' does not include the fetus in emancipation oaths. Note the two proofs given: dependent ownership and analogy to a limb.
The ruling is definitional, not a typical hukm taklifi.
2
Analyze the two proofs
First proof: The fetus is owned taba'an (dependently) to the mother, not maqsudan (independently). Second proof: It is like a limb (juz'), and the word 'slave' applies to whole persons, not parts. Confirm these by noting that the fetus cannot be sold separately and its emancipation does not suffice for expiation.
3
Examine the four scenarios
The author gives four images (suwar) to test the ruling. For each, determine whether the mother is freed and whether the fetus is freed. The key factor is whether the wording is general ('every slave') or restricted ('male slave', 'two slaves', or an exception).
In the first scenario (general 'every slave'), the mother is freed and the fetus is freed as a dependent. In the other three, the fetus is not freed because the wording is restricted.
4
Understand the linguistic principle
The text explains that 'mamluk' (masculine) is general and includes females. The restriction to males in an oath removes the mother and thus the fetus is not freed. The fetus is never an independent 'mamluk' at the time of the oath.
This is based on usul: al-amm includes all individuals of the category; takhsis removes some.
5
Apply the ruling to a new case
Given a similar scenario, use the reasoning to decide if the fetus is freed. For example, if the master says 'my female slaves are free' and he has a pregnant slave, the fetus is freed because the mother is included. If he says 'my male slaves are free', the fetus of a pregnant female is not freed.
This demonstrates the importance of wording (sighah) in oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمملوك لا يتناول الحمل لأن اللفظ يتناول المملوك المطلق والجنين مملوك تبعاً للأم لا مقصوداً، ولأنه عضو من وجه واسم المملوك يتناول الأنفس دون الأعضاء ولهذا لا يملك بيعه منفرداً ولا يجزئ عتقه عن الكفارة، فلو قال كل مملوك لي وله حمل أوصى له به دون أمه، أو قال كل مملوك لي ذكر فهو حر وله جارية حامل فولدت ذكراً لأقل من ستة أشهر، أو قال إن اشتريت مملوكين فهما حران فاشترى جارية حاملاً فإن الحمل في هذه الصور الثلاث لا يعتق لما ذكرنا، ولا تعتق الأم في المسألة الثانية أيضاً لتقييده بالذكورة، لا مسألة الثالثة كما في البدائع لأن شرط الحنث شراء مملوكين والحمل لا مملوكاً على الإطلاق، وكذا لو قال للحامل كل مملوك لي غيرك حر لم يعتق الحمل كما في المحيط. وإنما قيدنا بالصور الأربع لأنه لو قال كل مملوك لي حر وله جارية حاملة فإن الحامل تدخل فيعتق الحمل تبعاً لها كما في الهداية وهذا بناء على أن لفظة مملوك إما لذات متصفة بالمملوكية وقيد التذكير ليس جزء المفهوم، وإذا كان التأنيث جزء مفهوم مملوكة فيكون مملوك أعم من مملوكة فالثابت فيه عدم الدلالة على التأنيث لا الدلالة على عدم التأنيث. وأما أن الاستعمال استمر فيه على الأعمية فوجب اعتباره كذلك؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
And the slave [word] does not include the fetus because the word encompasses an absolute slave, and the fetus is a slave dependently to the mother, not independently; and because it is a limb in a way, and the name 'slave' applies to persons not limbs, and for this reason one cannot sell it separately, nor is its emancipation valid for expiation. So if he said, 'Every slave of mine, and he has a fetus, bequeaths to it without its mother'; or he said, 'Every male slave of mine is free', and he has a pregnant female slave, and she gave birth to a male before six months; or he said, 'If I buy two slaves, they are free', and he bought a pregnant female slave — in these three instances the fetus is not set free for what we mentioned; and the mother is not set free in the second instance due to restriction to males; and not in the third instance as in al-Bada'i' because the condition of the breach is buying two slaves, and the fetus is not an absolute slave. Similarly, if he said to a pregnant woman, 'Every slave of mine except you is free', the fetus is not set free as in al-Muhit. And we only restricted to four images because if he said, 'Every slave of mine is free', and he has a pregnant female slave, then the pregnant one enters and the fetus is set free dependently to her as in al-Hidayah, and this is based on that the word 'mamluk' is for an entity described by being owned, and the restriction of masculinity is not part of the concept; and when femininity is part of the concept of 'mamlukah', then 'mamluk' is more general than 'mamlukah', so what is established is the lack of indication of femininity, not the indication of lack of femininity. And usage is consistent with generality, so it must be regarded as such; as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that the term 'mamluk' (slave) in an oath of emancipation does not include a fetus (haml). The author provides several reasons: the fetus is owned dependently (taba'an) to its mother, not independently (maqsudan); it is analogous to a limb, and the word 'slave' applies to whole persons, not parts. This is proven by the fact that a fetus cannot be sold separately and its emancipation does not suffice for expiation. The author then gives four scenarios: (1) If someone says 'Every slave of mine is free' and he has a pregnant slave, the mother is freed and the fetus is freed with her (because the general term applies). (2) If he restricts to males and the pregnant slave gives birth to a male within six months, the mother is not freed (due to the restriction), nor is the fetus because it was not independently owned at the time of the oath; it is born after the oath but was a part of the mother. (3) If he says 'If I buy two slaves they are free' and buys a pregnant slave, the fetus is not freed because it is not a complete slave. (4) If he says 'Every slave except you' to a pregnant slave, the fetus is not freed. The key distinction is that when the word 'mamluk' is unqualified, it is general and includes females and fetuses dependently; when qualified as 'male' or otherwise, the restriction applies only to the independent slave.

Key Terms
المملوك (al-mamluk): A human being owned as property, legally capable of being bought and sold, and whose emancipation is valid.
الحمل (al-haml): The unborn offspring in the womb, which follows the mother in ownership but is not an independent legal entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth scenario, a man says to his pregnant female slave: 'Every slave of mine except you is free.' According to the passage, what is the status of the fetus?
A) The fetus is freed because it is not included in the exception.
B) The fetus is not freed because it is not an independent slave.
C) The fetus is freed only if it is male.
D) The fetus is freed because the mother is excepted.

Key Takeaway
The word 'mamluk' in an emancipation oath does not independently cover the fetus; the fetus is freed only if the mother is freed under an unqualified term.

Emancipation Upon Death: The Mudabbir and the Third
Ruling that slaves owned at time of oath become mudabbir and are freed from third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a slave to become mudabbir through a deathbed oath and distinguish the majority Hanafi view from Abu Yusuf's.

Core Idea
An oath of posthumous emancipation only covers slaves owned at the time of the oath, not those acquired later.

When a master declares, 'Every slave of mine is free after my death,' he creates a tadbir (posthumous emancipation). But which slaves are included? The Hanafi school answers that only those owned at the moment of the oath become mudabbir, and they are freed from the one-third of the estate. Imam Abu Yusuf differed, arguing that the language 'my slaves' refers to present ownership only, so later acquisitions are excluded. This dispute illustrates how legal reasoning (talil) applies to conditional declarations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
The act is an oath of emancipation after death: e.g., 'Every slave of mine is free after my death.' This is a conditional declaration that seeks to free slaves posthumously.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the majority), the slaves owned at the time of the oath become mudabbir (posthumously freed) and are freed from the one-third of the estate upon the master's death. Slaves acquired after the oath are not included.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held that only those slaves owned on the day the oath was sworn are freed; later acquisitions are not covered, because the word 'mamluk' (owned) linguistically refers to the present.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority position within the school.
4
Analyze the majority's evidence
The majority treat the oath as both a declaration of emancipation and a bequest (wasiyyah). Since bequests are limited to the one-third and are evaluated at the time of the bequest, the relevant ownership is at the time of the oath, not at the time of death.
This reasoning is based on the talil (causal link) that the oath creates a tadbir, which is akin to a will.
5
Apply the ruling
If a master owns two slaves at the time of the oath and later acquires a third, only the first two are freed. The third is not affected, regardless of the master's intention.
The slave authorized to trade (ma'dhun) is included in the general oath, as clarified in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبموته عتق من ملكه من ثلثه أيضاً) أي بموت المولى يعتق من كان في ملكه للحال بعد من ثلث المال. فالحاصل أن من كان في ملكه وقت اليمين مدبر مطلق أو بمدير مطلق وإنما هو مدير مقيد فيعتقان بموت المولى عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف : يعتق من كان في ملکه يوم حلف ولا يعتق ما استفاده بعد يمينه لأن اللفظ حقيقة للحال على ما بينا فلا يعتق به ما سيملكه ولهذا صار هو مدبراً دون الآخر. ولهما أن هذا إيجاب عتق وإيصاء حتى اعتبر من الثلث وفي الوصايا تعتبر الحالة المنتظرة والحالة الراهنة ألا قوله : (وبه علم أن ما في المجتبى (الخ أقول : الذي رأيته في المجتبى: ولا يدخل العبد المشترك والعبد الموهوب والمأذون في التجارة يعتق اهـ. فقوله والعبد الموهوب» بالواو والباء آخره من
His saying: (And upon his death, whoever he owns from his third is freed) means that upon the master's death, whoever was in his possession at the time of the oath is freed from the third of the estate. So the summary is that whoever was in his possession at the time of the oath is a mudabbir absolutely, or is mudabbir conditionally, and they are freed upon the master's death according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: only those in his possession on the day he swore are freed; what he acquired after the oath is not freed because the word is literally for the present state, as we explained, so it does not free what he will own; therefore the former becomes mudabbir, the latter does not. Their evidence is that this is a declaration of freedom and a bequest (wasiyyah), so it is considered from the one-third; and in bequests, both the expected state and the current state are considered. Indeed, his saying: (And from this it is known that what is in al-Mujtaba... I say: What I saw in al-Mujtaba: 'The co-owned slave, the gifted slave, and the slave authorized in trade are not included' end quote. So his saying 'and the gifted slave' is with a waw and a ba' at the end.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning emancipation oaths that take effect after the master's death. When someone says, 'Every slave of mine is free after my death,' the slaves owned at the time of the oath become mudabbir (posthumously freed). Upon death, they are freed from the one-third of the estate, similar to a bequest (wasiyyah). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that all slaves owned at the time of the oath are freed, even if the master acquires more later; the latter are not freed. Abu Yusuf argues that only those owned at the time of the oath are freed, and any acquired after are not, because the word 'mamluk' is for the present. The reasoning of the majority is that the oath is both an emancipation and a bequest, and in bequests, the relevant state is the time of the bequest (the oath), not future acquisition. The passage ends with a textual note correcting what is in al-Mujtaba, clarifying that the authorized slave is included in general oaths.

Key Terms
المدبر (al-mudabbir): A slave declared free upon the death of the master; the declaration is valid from the master's one-third of the estate.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest directing that property or freedom be given after death; here applied to emancipation from the one-third.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the majority’s ruling that only slaves owned at the time of the oath are freed from the one-third?
A) The word 'mamluk' literally refers to present ownership.
B) The oath is treated as a bequest (wasiyyah), so ownership at the time of the bequest is considered.
C) Consensus of the Companions.
D) It is based on a hadith.

Key Takeaway
A general oath of posthumous emancipation creates tadbir only for slaves owned at the moment of the oath, not those acquired later, and they are freed from the one-third of the estate.

Ruling on the Phrase 'Every Lawful Thing is Forbidden'
Explains the legal consequences of the common oath phrase 'كل حل علي حرام'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi ruling for the phrase 'كل حل علي حرام' and distinguish it from the Zahir al-Riwayah position, applying the role of custom (`urf) in determining the legal effect.

Core Idea
‘Every lawful thing is forbidden to me’ is a divorce oath if you have a wife; otherwise, an oath by Allah.

The phrase 'كل حل علي حرام' (every lawful thing is forbidden to me) is a common Arabic expression whose legal effect depends on marital status and customary usage. In the Hanafi school, there is an intra-madhhab dispute between the Zahir al-Riwayah (which treats it as an oath by Allah) and the mufta bihi (which treats it as a divorce oath if the speaker has a wife). This lesson unpacks that dispute and the role of `urf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its context
The phrase 'كل حل علي حرام' is an oath (yamin). Its legal effect depends on whether the speaker has a wife at the time of utterance.
The literal meaning is 'every lawful thing is forbidden to me'.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
According to the core transmitted texts (Zahir al-Riwayah), this phrase is considered an oath by Allah. Breaking it (e.g., by eating or drinking) requires expiation (kaffarah), and no divorce occurs.
This is based on the literal interpretation: forbidding lawful things is a type of oath.
3
State the mufta bihi position
According to the operative fatwa (mufta bihi) based on later scholars, if the speaker has a wife, the phrase is treated as an oath by divorce: one revocable divorce (talaq raj‘i) occurs immediately, and no sin or expiation for eating/drinking. If he has no wife, it remains an oath by Allah requiring expiation.
This change is due to customary usage (`urf) where men commonly use this phrase to mean divorce.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: under Zahir al-Riwayah, a man with a wife is not divorced but must pay expiation if he eats/drinks. Under mufta bihi, he is divorced and no expiation is needed. Also, for multiple wives, the views differ on whether all are divorced or only one.
The mufta bihi is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
5
Explain the role of custom in the tarjih
Later scholars gave weight to `urf (customary practice). Since in common usage only men swear by this phrase concerning their wives, it is understood as a divorce oath. This overrides the literal interpretation of an oath by Allah.
The text explicitly states: 'It has become a customary divorce.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتعارف سئل عن نيته وفيما ينصرف بلا نية لو قال أردت غيره لا يصدقه القاضي وفيما بينه وبين الله تعالى وهو مصدق ؛ هكذا قال في فتح القدير والحاصل أنه على ظاهر الرواية يحنث بالأكل والشرب فقط ولا يقع عليه طلاق، وعلى المفتى به إن لم يكن له امرأة فكذلك، وإن كان له امرأة وقع الطلاق عليها ولا يحنث بالأكل والشرب. وفي الظهيرية: رجل قال كل حل علي حرام أو قال كل حلال علي حرام أو قال حلال الله أو قال حلال المسلمين وله امرأة ولم ينو شيئاً قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل والفقيه أبو جعفر وأبو بكر الاسكاف وأبو بكر بن سعيد تبين امرأته بتطليقة وإن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث، وإن قال لم أنو الطلاق لا يصدق قضاء لأنه صار طلاقاً عرفاً ولهذا لا يحلف به إلا الرجال فإن كان له امرأة واحدة تبين بتطليقة، وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة، وإن حلف بهذا اللفظ إن كان فعل كذا وقد كان فعل وله امرأة واحدة أو أكثرين جميعاً وإن لم يكن له امرأة لا يلزمه شيء لأنه جعل يميناً بالطلاق، ولو جعلناه يميناً بالله فهو غموس. وإن حلف بهذا على أمر في المستقبل ففعل ذلك وليس له امرأة كان عليه الكفارة لأن تحريم الحلال يمين، وإن كان له امرأة وقت اليمين فماتت قبل الشرط أو بانت لا إلى عدة ثم باشر الشرط لا تلزمه الكفارة لأن يمينه انصرف إلى الطلاق وقت وجودها وإن لم تكن له امرأة وقت اليمين ثم تزوج امرأة ثم باشر الشرط اختلفوا فيه قال الفقيه أبو جعفر : تبين المتزوجة وقال غيره : لا تبين وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى لأن يمينه جعل يميناً بالله تعالى وقت وجودها فلا يكون طلاقاً بعد ذلك اهـ. وقيد بصيغة العموم لأنه لو قال لزوجته أنت علي حرام فقد قدم في باب الإيلاء أنه ينصرف للزوجة فتطلق من غير نية. جيد موافق لكلام المتقدمين ويحمل كلام صاحب الهداية وغيره على ما إذا لم يكن الاستعمال مشتركاً فيه وفي غيره، أما إذا كان مشتركاً تعين موافقة المتقدمين. وأقول : أكثر عوام بلادنا لا يقصدون بقولهم أنت محرمة علي أو حرام علي أو حرمتك علي إلا حرمة الوطء المقابل لحله ولذلك أكثرهم يضرب مدة لتحريمها ولا يريد قطعاً إلا تحريم الجماع إلى هذه المدة، ولا شك أنه يمين موجب للإيلاء تأمل. فقل من حقق هذه المسألة على وجهها وانظر إلى قولهم لا نقول لا تشترط النية لكن يجعل ناوياً عرفاً فهو صريح في اعتبار العرف فإن لم يكن العرف كذلك بل كان مشتركاً تعين اعتبار النية وتصديق الحالف كما هو مذهب المتقدمين قوله : (وإن كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً يقع على كل واحدة واحدة بائنة) قال في النهر بعده لكن في الدراية : لو كان له امرأتان وقع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان في الأظهر كقوله امرأتي كذا وله امرأتان أو أكثر ا.هـ. قال محشي مسكين: ظاهر قوله «أو أكثر أن وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان لا يخص الثنتين بل كذلك لو كن ثلاثاً أو أربعاً فهو قول مقابل لما في الظهيرية، وحيث كان وقوع الطلاق على واحدة وإليه البيان هو الأظهر مطلقاً، سواء كان له امرأتان أو أكثر، فما في الظهيرية من وقوعه على الكل خلاف الأظهر وإن كان في ذلك البحر لم يحك خلافه ولم يذكر ما نقله في النهر عن الدراية ا هـ . قلت : لم يذكره اعتماداً على ما قدمه آخر باب الإيلاء وقدم هناك عن الفتح أن الأشبه ما هنا لأن قوله حلال الله أو حلال المسلمين يعم كل زوجة على سبيل الاستغراق .
It is disputed: he is asked about his intention. And in what is understood without intention, if he says 'I intended something else', the judge does not believe him, but between him and Allah, he is believed. Thus said in Fath al-Qadir. The conclusion is that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, he is sinful (by breaking the oath) only by eating and drinking, and no divorce occurs. According to the mufta bihi position, if he has no wife, the same; if he has a wife, divorce occurs and he is not sinful by eating or drinking. In al-Zahiriyyah: A man says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' or 'every lawful thing is forbidden to me' or 'the lawful of Allah' or 'the lawful of the Muslims' while having a wife and intending nothing – the Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, al-Faqih Abu Ja'far, Abu Bakr al-Askaf, and Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id said: his wife becomes separated by one revocable divorce; if he intended three, then three. If he says 'I did not intend divorce', he is not believed judicially because it has become a customary divorce. Therefore only men swear by it. If he has one wife, she becomes separated by one divorce; if he has three or four, an irrevocable divorce falls on each. If he swears by this phrase on a past action and it occurred while he had one wife or many, all [wives] are divorced; if he has no wife, nothing is required because he made it an oath by divorce. If we considered it an oath by Allah, it would be a false oath. If he swears by this on a future matter, then does it, and has no wife, he owes expiation because forbidding the lawful is an oath. If he had a wife at the time of the oath, then she died or became separated without a revocable period before the condition, then he fulfills the condition, no expiation is required because his oath was directed to divorce at the time of its existence. If he had no wife at the time of the oath, then married a woman, then fulfilled the condition, they differed: al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said the new wife becomes separated; others said no, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth adopted this and fatwa is upon it because his oath is considered an oath by Allah at the time of its existence, so it is not divorce thereafter. End. And it is restricted by the general form because if he said to his wife 'you are forbidden to me', it was stated in the chapter of Ila' that it is directed to the wife, so she is divorced without intention. Good, agreeing with the early scholars, and the statement of the author of al-Hidayah and others is interpreted as when the usage is not shared between [food and wife]; if it is shared, then agreement with the early scholars is determined. I say: most common people in our land do not intend by their saying 'you are forbidden to me' or 'haram upon me' or 'I forbid you to me' except the prohibition of intercourse corresponding to its permissibility. Therefore most of them set a period for its prohibition and definitely intend only prohibition of intercourse for that period. There is no doubt that it is an oath necessitating Ila'. So few have investigated this issue properly. Look at their statement: 'We do not say that intention is not required, but he is considered as intending customarily.' This is explicit in considering custom. If custom is not like that but rather shared, then intention is taken into account and the oath-taker is believed, as is the madhhab of the early scholars. His statement: 'if he has three or four, an irrevocable divorce falls on each.' In al-Nahr after it: but in al-Dirayah: if he has two wives, divorce falls on one, and clarification is referred to him in the apparent view, like his saying 'my wife is such' while having two wives or more. End. The commentator Miskin said: the apparent meaning of his statement 'or more' is that divorce falling on one and his clarification is not specific to two but similarly if they are three or four, so it is a statement opposing what is in al-Zahiriyyah. Since the occurrence of divorce on one and referral to clarification is the apparent view absolutely, whether he has two wives or more, so what is in al-Zahiriyyah of it occurring on all is contrary to the apparent view, even though in al-Bahr he did not mention the disagreement nor what al-Nahr transmitted from al-Dirayah. End. I say: he did not mention it relying on what he presented at the end of the chapter of Ila', and he presented there from al-Fath that the more appropriate is what is here because his saying 'the lawful of Allah' or 'the lawful of the Muslims' includes every wife in a general sense.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal effect of the common oath phrase 'كل حل علي حرام' (every lawful thing is forbidden to me). The text opens by noting a difference between the ruling according to Zahir al-Riwayah and the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) position. According to Zahir al-Riwayah, uttering this phrase only makes one sinful by eating or drinking (i.e., it is considered an oath by Allah, requiring expiation if broken), and no divorce occurs. According to the mufta bihi, if the speaker has a wife, the phrase is treated as an oath by divorce: divorce occurs immediately (one revocable divorce), and he is not sinful by eating or drinking because the oath is not considered an oath by Allah; if he has no wife, it remains an oath by Allah requiring expiation. The passage then reports from al-Zahiriyyah that the early Hanafi scholars (Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, Abu Ja'far, Abu Bakr al-Askaf, Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id) held that if a man with a wife says this without intention, his wife is divorced by one revocable divorce. If he intended three, it is three. He is not believed judicially if he claims no intention because it has become a customary divorce. The ruling distinguishes between past and future conditional oaths. For a past condition (e.g., 'if I did such-and-such'), if he had a wife at the time of the oath, the oath is effectively a divorce and all wives are divorced; if no wife, nothing is required because it is treated as an oath by divorce (which cannot exist without a wife), but if considered an oath by Allah it would be a false (intentional false) oath. For a future condition, if he has no wife at the oath time, he owes expiation (because it is an oath by Allah); if he had a wife who later dies or separates before the condition, no expiation because his oath was already directed to divorce. If he had no wife at oath time but later marries and then fulfills the condition, the stronger view (taken by Abu al-Layth and the fatwa) is that the new wife is not divorced; the oath is considered an oath by Allah at the time it was made, so it does not become a divorce later. The text then discusses the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' (you are forbidden to me), which is specific to the wife (not food/drink) and thus triggers Ila' or divorce depending on intention. Finally, the author comments on the importance of custom (`urf`): if the common usage of such phrases is exclusively for prohibiting intercourse, then intention is assumed; if usage is shared, the swearer's claimed intention is accepted. The passage ends with a note on multiple wives: according to al-Dirayah, if one says this and has multiple wives, the apparent view is that only one wife is divorced and the man can clarify which one, like the ruling on 'my wife is such'.

Key Terms
كل حل علي حرام (kullu hallin 'alayya haram): A common Arabic phrase meaning 'every lawful thing is forbidden to me', used as a form of oath, often implying divorce if one has a wife.
يمين بالطلاق (yamin bi al-talaq): An oath that is conditioned on divorce; if the condition is fulfilled, divorce takes effect. In this text, the phrase is considered a direct divorce oath, not conditional.
غموس (ghumus): A false oath sworn intentionally; in this context, if the phrase were considered an oath by Allah, but the person intended it as a divorce oath, it would be a false oath (i.e., a sin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a man with a wife says 'كل حل علي حرام' without any specific intention?
A) He is sinful for eating or drinking, and no divorce occurs.
B) His wife is divorced by one revocable divorce, and he is not sinful for eating or drinking.
C) He must pay expiation (kaffarah) for breaking the oath.
D) Both divorce and sin occur.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for 'كل حل علي حرام' treats it as a divorce oath if the speaker has a wife, based on `urf, but as an oath by Allah if he does not.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Ruling of 'You are Forbidden to Me' in Divorce
Clarifying the ruling of the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi fatwa that the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' results in divorce (talaq) when the husband’s understanding of 'haram' is divorce, and distinguish this from general prohibitions that include food and drink.

Core Idea
If a husband says 'أنت علي حرام' and his custom equates 'haram' with divorce, talaq occurs even without intention.

This lesson unpacks a specific Hanafi fatwa regarding the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' (you are forbidden to me). The passage clarifies that this phrase is directed specifically to the wife and, if the husband understands 'haram' as divorce, divorce takes place even without explicit intention. We will explore the reasoning, contrast with general prohibitions, and see how this ruling is applied in marital contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its legal scope
Recognize that 'أنت علي حرام' is a direct address to the wife. Note that the Hanafi school treats this phrase as specific to the wife, not including other things like food or drink.
This specificity is crucial: a general prohibition ('كل حل علي حرام') would include food and drink.
2
Understand the fatwa on divorce without intention
The passage reports a fatwa: if the husband says this and his customary understanding of 'haram' is divorce, then talaq occurs even if he did not intend divorce. The apparent meaning ('mazann') suffices.
The fatwa relies on the husband's 'urf (custom) — if in his mind 'haram' means divorce, the utterance triggers talaq.
3
Contrast with other possible intentions
The earlier text from the chapter of Ila' lists multiple possibilities: if he intends prohibition (Ila'), it is Ila'; if he intends Zihar, it is Zihar; if he intends falsehood, it is false; if he intends divorce, it is ba'in; if he intends three, it is three. The fatwa focuses on the default when no specific intention is articulated but the husband's understanding equates 'haram' with divorce.
This shows the nuance: intention matters, but when it is absent, the common understanding of the term in the husband's mind governs.
4
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Suppose a husband in a heated argument says 'أنت علي حرام' without any explicit intention. If in his personal understanding (or the local custom) 'haram' means divorce, then a single revocable divorce occurs. The wife is divorced, and the husband must observe the rules of divorce (iddah, etc.).
This is the mufta bihi position according to the fatwa cited.
5
Distinguish from general prohibition statements
The passage explicitly contrasts with a general statement like 'كل حلال علي حرام' (all permissible is forbidden to me), which would include food and drink and is treated as a vow (nadhr), not divorce. The specificity of addressing the wife is key.
This distinction prevents confusion between divorce and vows.

Source Text

البحر لم يحك خلافه ولم يذكر ما نقله في النهر عن الدراية ا هـ . قلت : لم يذكره اعتماداً على ما قدمه آخر باب الإيلاء وقدم هناك عن الفتح أن الأشبه ما هنا . قوله : ( فقد قدم في باب الإيلاء أنه ينصرف للزوجة فتطلق من غير نية) كان عليه حذف قوله فتطلق من غير نية لأنه مساو في ذلك لقوله كل حل علي حرام على أن المذكور في المتن في باب الإيلاء هكذا: أنت علي حرام إيلاء إن نوى التحريم أو لم ينو شيئاً، وظهار إن نواه، وكذب إن نوى الكذب، وبائنة إن نوى الطلاق، وثلاث إن نوى. وفي الفتوى إذا قال لامرأته أنت علي حرام والحرام عنده طلاق ولكن لم ينو طلاقاً وقع الطلاق ا.هـ. وحاصله أن قوله أنت علي حرام يخص المرأة ولا يدخل فيه الطعام والشراب بخلاف العام قوله : (فعليه الوفاء بما سمى) قال الرملي : هذا صريح في تعينه وعدم جواز البدل. هذا مع تصريحهم بعدم تعين الدرهم، ولا شك أن الدينار كذلك وكذلك الفلوس النافقة لعدم التفاوت وسيأتي قريباً أنه يلزمه الوفاء بالأصل لا بكل وصف
Al-Bahr did not mention a difference nor what al-Nahr transmitted from al-Dirayah. I said: He did not mention it relying on what he presented at the end of the chapter of Ila', and he presented there from al-Fath that the most appropriate is here. His words: (He already presented in the chapter of Ila' that it is directed to the wife, so she is divorced without intention) he should have omitted his words 'so she is divorced without intention' because it is equal in that to his saying 'all permitted is forbidden to me' except that what is mentioned in the text in the chapter of Ila' is as follows: 'You are forbidden to me' is Ila' if he intends prohibition or did not intend anything, and Zihar if he intends it, and falsehood if he intends lying, and a divorce (ba'in) if he intends divorce, and three if he intends. And in the fatwa: If he says to his wife 'You are forbidden to me' and 'al-haram' in his view is divorce but he did not intend divorce, divorce occurs. Its conclusion: His saying 'You are forbidden to me' is specific to the wife and does not include food and drink, unlike the general. His words: (So he must fulfill what he specified) al-Ramli said: This is explicit in its determination and the impermissibility of substitution. This is along with their explicit statement that a dirham is not determined, and there is no doubt that a dinar is likewise, and so the current coins due to lack of disparity. Soon it will come that he is bound to fulfill the original, not every description.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a specific phrase in the context of vows and the chapter of Ila' (oath of abstinence from marital relations). The author, al-Bahr, is discussing the interpretation of the phrase 'أنت علي حرام' (you are forbidden to me). The passage references an earlier discussion in the chapter of Ila' where it was established that this phrase is directed to the wife and results in divorce even if the husband did not intend divorce, because the apparent meaning indicates prohibition. The fatwa reported states that if a man says this to his wife and 'al-haram' in his view means divorce, then talaq occurs even without intention. The conclusion is that this phrase is specific to the wife and does not include food and drink, unlike a general statement. The passage then returns to the main topic of vows, quoting al-Ramli who says that the matn's wording 'so he must fulfill what he specified' is clear that the specified vow must be fulfilled without substitution, just as a dirham or dinar is determined (not just any currency).

Key Terms
حرام (haram): In this context, the word is understood by the speaker to mean 'divorce' or 'prohibition of marital relations', leading to talaq.
إيلاء (ila'): An oath by a husband to abstain from marital relations with his wife for a period.
ظهار (zihar): A husband saying to his wife that she is like his mother's back, which is a form of divorce that requires expiation.

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs because intention is required.
B) The phrase is interpreted as Ila', not divorce.
C) Divorce (talaq) occurs even without explicit intention.
D) The marriage becomes void automatically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa on 'أنت علي حرام' demonstrates the power of custom ('urf) in overriding literal intent when the phrase is culturally understood as divorce.

Oath to Enter a House Under Compulsion and Attribution
Rulings on oaths to enter a house when compelled by wind, animal, or human, and attribution of the house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on compulsion (ikrah) to determine whether an oath to enter a house is broken, distinguishing between involuntary and voluntary entry, and attribute the house correctly based on ownership, lease, loan, or residence.

Core Idea
Forced entry does not break an oath; only voluntary re-entry after freedom of choice breaks it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses a key nuance in the law of oaths: when a person swears not to enter a house, but is compelled to enter by wind, animal, or human force. The ruling turns on whether the entry was truly voluntary. You will learn how the Hanafi school handles these scenarios and how attribution of the house matters, including cases where the house is occupied by a third party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and act
Start by recognizing the basic scenario: a person swears an oath not to enter a specific house. The legal question is whether a particular entry constitutes a breach of that oath.
The oath may be by Allah, divorce, or manumission.
2
Distinguish compelled entry scenarios
The passage lists three types of compulsion: stumbling, wind, and a bolting animal. In each, if the person could not resist, the entry is considered involuntary and does not break the oath.
The key is the absence of choice.
3
Analyze human compulsion and re-entry
When a person is forced in by another human, then later leaves and re-enters voluntarily, there is a dispute. The relied-upon fatwa is that the subsequent voluntary entry does break the oath because the condition of free will is satisfied by the second entry.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the figurative meaning of 'entering'
The author notes that 'placing the foot' (wad' al-qadam) is treated as entering due to figurative usage (majaz). This is a Hanafi usul principle applied to oaths.
This affects cases where only part of the body enters.
5
Determine house attribution
The latter part of the passage addresses how to attribute a house to a person. If the oath specifies 'Zayd's house,' it includes any house Zayd occupies by ownership, lease, or loan. Entering a house that is merely rented out (dar ghalla) by Zayd does not break the oath unless evidence indicates otherwise. Entering a daughter's house even if she lives with her husband breaks the oath.
Attribution is based on current residence, not necessarily ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج الظهيرية : ولو جاء إلى بابها وهو يشتد في المشي أي يعدو فانعثر أو انزلق فوقع في الدار اختلفوا فيه، والصحيح أنه لا يحنث وإن دفعته الريح وأوقعته في الدار اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يحنث إن كان لا يستطيع الامتناع وإن كان على دابة فجمحت وانفلتت وأدخلته في الدار وهو لا يستطيع إمساكها لا يحنث، وإن أدخله إنسان مكرهاً فخرج منها ثم دخل بعد ذلك مختاراً اختلفوا فيه والفتوى على أنه يحنث .اهـ. ووجهه أن الشرط لم يوجد بالدخول مكرهاً بدليل عدم الحنث وقد وجد بالدخول ثانياً مختاراً فحنث وسيأتي بعد ذلك إيضاحه. ووضع القدم كالدخول فيما ذكرنا لأنه صار مجازاً عن الدخول وهي مسألة الحقيقة والمجاز في الأصول. وهذا كله باعتبار الدار، وأما باعتبار صفتها بالإضافة إلى فلان فإنه يحنث إذا دخل داراً مضافة إلى فلان، سواء كان يسكتها بالملك أو بالإجارة أو بالعارية. وفي المجتبى : لو قال إن دخلت دار زيد فعبدي حر وإن دخلت دار عمرو فامرأتي طالق فدخل دار زيد وهي في يد عمرو بإجارة يعتق وتطلق إذا لم ينو فإن نوى شيئاً صدق اهـ. وفي المحيط : لو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان وله دار يسكنها ودار غلة فدخل دار الغلة لا يحنث إذا لم يدل الدليل على دار الغلة وغيرها لأن داره مطلقاً دار يسكنها .اهـ. وفي الخانية: لو حلف لا يدخل دار ابنته وابنته تسكن في دار زوجها أو حلف لا يدخل دار أمه وأمه تسكن في بيت زوجها فدخل الحالف حنث .اهـ.
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths Regarding Entering and Exiting: In al-Zahiriyya: If he came to its door while walking fast, that is, running, and stumbled or slipped and fell into the house, they differed about it, and the correct view is that he does not break his oath. And if the wind pushed him and threw him into the house, they differed about it, and the correct view is that he does not break his oath if he cannot resist. And if he was on an animal that bolted and broke loose and brought him into the house while he could not hold it, he does not break his oath. And if a person forced him in, then he came out and then entered afterwards voluntarily, they differed about it, and the fatwa is that he breaks his oath. The end. And its reasoning is that the condition was not met by forced entry, as evidenced by the absence of breaking the oath, and it was met by the second voluntary entry, so he breaks his oath. And it will be clarified later. Placing the foot is like entering in what we mentioned, because it has become figuratively used for entering, and this is a matter of literal and figurative meaning in usul. All this is with regard to the house. As for its attribute of attribution to so-and-so, he breaks his oath if he enters a house attributed to so-and-so, whether he occupies it by ownership, lease, or loan. In al-Mujtaba: If he said, 'If I enter Zayd's house, my slave is free, and if I enter Amr's house, my wife is divorced,' then he entered Zayd's house while it was in Amr's possession by lease, he frees and divorces if he did not intend; if he intended something, his claim is believed. The end. In al-Muhit: If he swore not to enter so-and-so's house, and he has a house he lives in and a rental house, and he entered the rental house, he does not break his oath unless evidence indicates the rental house or otherwise, because his house absolutely is the one he lives in. The end. In al-Khaniyya: If he swore not to enter his daughter's house, and his daughter lives in her husband's house, or he swore not to enter his mother's house, and his mother lives in her husband's house, then the swearer entered, he breaks his oath. The end.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings on oaths related to entering a house, particularly under compulsion and attribution. It opens with a discussion from al-Zahiriyya: if someone is compelled to enter a house—whether by stumbling, wind, or a bolting animal—and has no ability to resist, he does not break his oath because the entry was involuntary. However, if a person forces him in, then he later re-enters voluntarily, he does break the oath, and this is the fatwa. The reasoning is that the condition for breaking the oath was not fulfilled by the forced entry, but was fulfilled by the later voluntary entry. The author then notes that 'placing the foot' (wad' al-qadam) is treated like entering because it is a figurative expression for entering, referring to the Hanafi principle of literal vs. figurative meaning in legal theory. Next, the discussion shifts to the attribution of the house: if the house is attributed to someone (e.g., 'Zayd's house'), the swearer breaks the oath upon entering regardless of whether the person owns, leases, or borrows it. Specific examples from al-Mujtaba, al-Muhit, and al-Khaniyya illustrate this: if the house is in the possession of another by lease, the oath is still broken; if one has a residence and a rental property, entering the rental does not break the oath unless there is evidence; and entering a daughter's or mother's house even when they live with their husbands still breaks the oath.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, specifically a binding vow to Allah.
الدخول (al-dukhul): Entering, specifically moving from outside to inside a house.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compulsion, being forced to act against one's will.
وضع القدم (wad' al-qadam): Placing the foot, treated figuratively as entering.
دار غلة (dar ghalla): Rental property that generates income.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because it is not her exclusive house.
B) Yes, he breaks his oath.
C) Only if he stays overnight.
D) Only if he enters with permission.

Key Takeaway
Voluntary entry after compelled exit breaks an oath; forced entry does not.

Oaths with Definite and Indefinite Collective Nouns
Rulings on oaths using collective nouns and whether speaking to one individual breaks the oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths using definite and indefinite collective nouns, apply the rule to determine when a single act breaks the oath, and distinguish the role of intention and custom.

Core Idea
A definite plural in an oath applies to one individual; indefinite plural also to one; intention can override for definite but not indefinite.

In Hanafi fiqh, the interpretation of collective nouns in oaths follows the principle of genus (ism jins). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores whether speaking to one person breaks an oath involving a plural noun. The answer depends on whether the noun is definite or indefinite, and whether the swearer intended the whole group. The ruling is supported by Quranic usage and practical examples such as eating and drinking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the noun type
First, determine if the noun in the oath is a definite plural (with 'al'), indefinite singular/plural, or a genus noun. This affects whether one individual suffices to break the oath.
Definite plurals like 'الفقراء' are ism jins.
2
Apply the default rule
For a definite plural, the oath is broken by a single act unless the swearer intended the entire group. The basis is that the purpose is to avoid the sworn thing, and one cannot avoid the whole genus, so it defaults to the minimum.
Cites Quran 'لا يحل لك النساء'.
3
Consider exceptions for consumables
If the oath is about consuming something (eating/drinking), the entire quantity must be consumed if it is finite (e.g., a cup of water); for an infinite quantity (e.g., sea water), a drop suffices.
Distinguishes between 'water of this cup' and 'water of this sea'.
4
Evaluate intention
If the swearer intended the whole group, he is believed judicially and religiously for definite nouns. For indefinite nouns (e.g., 'رجلاً'), an intention to specify a particular individual is not accepted.
Definite: 'الرجل' allows specifying; indefinite: 'رجلاً' does not.
5
Account for custom
In some contexts, custom may treat a plural as meaning a single individual (e.g., 'أقاربك' relatives). The mufti must consider 'urf when it contradicts the default rule.
Quote from al-Qunyah: 'إن أحسنت إلى أقاربك' breaks by one in custom.

وقد وقعت حادثة أن رجلاً حلف بالطلاق أن أولاد زوجته لا يطلعون إلى بيته فطلع واحد هل يحنث ؟ فأجبت بأنه لا يحنث ولا بد من الجمع لأنه جمع ليس فيه الألف واللام قال في الواقعات : إذا قال والله لا أكلم الفقراء أو المساكين أو الرجال فكلم واحداً منهم بحنث لأنه اسم جنس بخلاف قوله رجالاً أو نساء هي (لا أكلم الفقراء أو المساكين (الخ) ولو قال إن كلمت بني آدم أو الرجال أو النساء حنث بالفرد إلا أن ينوي الكل إلحاقاً للجمع المعرف بالجنس لقوله تعالى لا يحل لك النساء ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٢] فإنه لا يختص بالجمع فإذا لم ينو حنث بالفرد لأن غرضه باليمين منع نفسه من المحلوف عليه وليس في وسعه إثبات كل الجنس فينصرف إلى ما دونه وذلك مجهول فصرفناه إلى الأدنى وهو الواحد لتيقنه . ولهذا لو حلف لا يشرب ماء هذا البحر ينصرف إلى قطرة منه وفي ماء هذا الكوز إلى جميعه، وفي لا يأكل هذا الطعام لا يحنث ما لم يأكله كله دفعة وإن لم يقدر يحنث بأكل بعضه، وفي رواية إن أمكنه أكله في عمره لا يحنث بالبعض والأول أصح. ولو كان مكان الأكل بيع لا يحنث بالبعض لأن البيع يرد على جميعه. هذا كله إذا لم ينو شيئاً، فلو نوى الكل صدق ديانة وقضاء. ولو قال إن كلمت الرجل فكلم رجلاً وقال عنيت باليمين غيره يصدق قضاء لأنه اسم جنس بخلاف إن كلمت رجلاً لأنه منكر فلا تصح نية التخصيص فيه . ولو قال لا أكل التمر أو تمراً أو الطعام أو طعاماً أو لا أشرب الماء أو ماء فإن المعرف والمنكر فيه سواء لكونه اسم جنس فيقع على الأدنى وإن كان منكراً وفي الجمع المنكر اهـ. فقد علمت أن الجمع المعرف بالألف واللام كالمفرد وغيره على حقيقته ولا تأثير للإضافة وعدمها بدليل ما في الواقعات أيضاً : لو قال والله لا أكلم إخوة فلان والأخ واحد، فإن كان يعلم يحنث إذا كلم ذلك الواحد لأنه ذكر الجمع وأراد الواحد وإن كان لا يعلم لا يحنث لأنه لم يرد الواحد فبقيت اليمين على الجمع كمن حلف لا يأكل ثلاثة أرغفة من هذا الحب وليس فيه إلا رغيف واحد وهو لا يعلم لا يحنث .اهـ. بلفظه وهو صريح في أن الجمع المضاف كالمنكر لكن قال في القنية : إن أحسنت إلى أقاربك فأنت طالق فأحسنت إلى واحد منهم يحنث ولا يراد الجمع في عرفنا .اهـ. فيحتاج إلى الفرق إلا أن يدعي أن في العرف فرقاً.
An incident occurred: a man swore by divorce that his wife's children would not look into his house, and one looked. Does he break his oath? I answered that he does not break his oath, and the plural (جمع) is necessary because it is a plural without the definite article (alif lam). He said in al-Waqi'at: If he said, 'By Allah, I will not speak to the poor or the needy or the men,' and he spoke to one of them, he breaks his oath because it is a genus noun, unlike his saying 'men' or 'women' (indefinite plural). If he said, 'If I speak to the sons of Adam, or the men, or the women,' he breaks his oath by one, unless he intends all, appending the definite plural to the genus, due to His statement (Surah al-Ahzab: 52) 'it is not lawful for you the women'—it is not specific to the plural. So if he does not intend, he breaks his oath by one, because his purpose in the oath is to prevent himself from the sworn thing, and it is not within his ability to prove the entire genus, so it applies to what is less than that, and that is unknown, so we apply it to the lowest, which is the one, due to certainty. For this reason, if he swore not to drink the water of this sea, it applies to a drop from it, and for the water of this cup, to all of it. And if he swore not to eat this food, he does not break his oath until he eats all of it at once; if he cannot, he breaks his oath by eating part. In one narration, if he can eat it in his lifetime, he does not break his oath by part, and the first is more correct. If instead of eating it were selling, he does not break his oath by part, because sale applies to the whole. This is all if he did not intend anything; if he intended the whole, he is believed religiously and judicially. If he said, 'If I speak to the man,' and he spoke to a man and said, 'I meant by the oath someone else,' he is believed judicially because it is a genus noun, unlike if he said 'If I speak to a man' because it is indefinite, so an intention of specification is not valid. If he said, 'I will not eat dates, or a date, or food, or a food, or I will not drink water, or a water,' then the definite and indefinite are equal because it is a genus noun, so it applies to the lowest, even if indefinite. And for the indefinite plural. The end. So you have learned that the definite plural with alif lam is like the singular and other than it according to its reality, and attribution or lack thereof has no effect, as evidenced by what is in al-Waqi'at also: If he said, 'By Allah, I will not speak to the brothers of so-and-so,' and the brother is one, then if he knows, he breaks his oath when he speaks to that one because he mentioned the plural and intended the one; if he does not know, he does not break his oath because he did not intend the one, so the oath remains on the plural, like one who swore not to eat three loaves from this stack, and there is only one loaf in it, and he does not know, he does not break his oath. The end, verbatim. And it is explicit that the plural with attribution is like the indefinite. But he said in al-Qunyah: 'If I do good to your relatives, you are divorced,' and she did good to one of them, she breaks her oath, and the plural is not intended in our custom. The end. So a distinction is needed unless one claims that there is a difference in custom.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on how collective nouns (جمع) function in oaths. It begins with a real incident: a man swore by divorce that his wife's children would not look into his house; one child looked. The answer given is that he does not break his oath because the word 'children' is a plural without the definite article (ال), and therefore it requires the plural meaning, not a single child. The passage then quotes al-Waqi'at providing general rules: if someone swears not to speak to 'the poor' (with al) and speaks to one poor person, he breaks the oath because it is a genus noun (اسم جنس) that includes the individual. Similarly, if he swears not to speak to 'men' (جمع معرف) he breaks by one. This is supported by the Quranic verse (Surah al-Ahzab 52) where 'women' (النساء) is used generally. The principle is that the purpose of the oath is to prevent oneself from the sworn thing; since one cannot avoid the entire genus, the oath applies to the least possible, i.e., one individual. This is consistent with the Hanafi rule that indefinite plurals (جمع منكر) also apply to the lowest. Exceptions: if the object is something that can be consumed entirely (like a cup of water), the oath requires all of it; but for a large quantity (like sea water), a single drop suffices. The passage also discusses intention: if the swearer intends the whole genus, he is believed religiously and judicially. However, if the noun is indefinite (like 'a man'), an intention to specify a particular individual is not accepted. The passage ends with a quote from al-Qunyah noting that in custom, the plural 'relatives' is understood to mean one relative, so a distinction is needed between custom and the general rule.

Key Terms
الجمع المعرف بالألف واللام (al-jam' al-mu'arrif bi al-alif wa al-lam): A plural noun with the definite article 'al', e.g., 'الرجال'.
اسم جنس (ism jins): A genus noun that encompasses all individuals of a category.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention of the swearer, which can specify the scope of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'By Allah, I will not speak to the poor' (الفقراء). He then speaks to one poor person. Does he break his oath?
A) No, because he must speak to all the poor.
B) Yes, because 'the poor' is a genus noun and applies to one.
C) Only if he spoke to them all at once.
D) Only if he intended the specific poor person.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a definite plural in an oath applies to a single individual by default, and only intention or custom can specify the whole group.

Oaths of Entry: Sequential Consequences and Marriage Timing
Rulings on sequential slave freeing and Kufa entry oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state how the wording of an oath (wa, fa, thumma) affects the timing of a condition and the occurrence of hanth.

Core Idea
The conjunction between entry and marriage determines whether marriage must precede, follow, or be simultaneous with entry to avoid hanth.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq compiles multiple rulings on oaths involving entry into places. The first part illustrates a scenario where a man's three statements free all his slaves sequentially due to his claim of falsehood. Then, the lesson focuses on oaths conditioned on entering Kufa and marriage, where subtle differences in Arabic conjunctions completely change the required sequence to avoid breaking the oath. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi oath law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath Structure
Read the first ruling about the three statements. Note that the speaker claims he is lying in all three, so freeing all slaves becomes necessary. Then focus on the Kufa examples. Identify the three variations: 'wa lam atazawwaj', 'fa lam atazawwaj', and 'thumma lam atazawwaj'.
The 'wa' indicates a state simultaneous with entry; 'fa' indicates immediate sequence; 'thumma' indicates delayed sequence without a time limit.
2
Determine the Condition
For each phrasing, ascertain whether the marriage condition refers to before entry, at the moment of entry, or after entry. The text explicitly states each ruling.
With 'wa', marriage must be before entry; with 'fa', marriage must occur at the time of entry; with 'thumma', marriage must occur after entry but can be at any time forever.
3
Apply the Hananef Principle
The Hanafi position is that the conjunction dictates the temporal relationship. Hanth occurs only if the condition is violated. Use the given explanations to confirm when entry triggers hanth.
The principle of 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' applies here: the wording reflects the intention.
4
Distinguish from Other Rulings
Compare with the subsequent rulings about house entry. Note that the same logic applies to other contexts: the particle 'aw' can function like 'hatta' in sequential oaths.
The lesson from al-Idda is especially important: 'aw' means 'until' in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج والوسط عتق بالكلام الثالث وعتق الثالث بعتق الأول، لأن الحالف زعم أنه كاذب في الكل فيلزمه عتق الكل. ولو قال إن دخلت الكوفة ولم أتزوج فعبدي حر، فإن دخل قبل التزوج حنث، ولو قال فلم أتزوج فهذا على أن يكون التزوج بعد الدخول حين يدخل. ولو قال إن دخلت الكوفة ثم لم أتزوج فهو على أن يتزوج بعد الدخول على الأبد ا هـ. وفي القنية : كان في البيت الشتوي فخاصم امرأته فقال إن دخلت هذا البيت إلى العيد فالحلال عليه حرام، ثم قال نويت ذلك البيت بعينه يصدق. حلف لا يدخل على هؤلاء القوم ثم دخل عتبة الباب فرأى واحداً منهم فرجع لا يحنث ا هـ. وفي الخلاصة: قال لامرأته إن دخلت دار أبيك فكل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق فدخل دار أبيها ثم إنها حرمت عليه فتزوجها لا تطلق بتلك اليمين لأنها معرفة بإضافة اليمين فلا تدخل تحت النكرة؛ هذا في مجموع النوازل. وفي النوازل : قال لامرأته إن دخلت الدار فنسائي طوالق فدخلت الدار وقع الطلاق عليها وعلى غيرها والاعتماد على هذا دون ما ذكر في مجموع النوازل، ولو قال لامرأته إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق بغير خسران يشترط قبولها عند دخول الدار وتفسير غير الخسران إن وهبت المهر ثم دخلت الدار ا.هـ. وفي العمدة لو قال لا أدع فلاناً يدخل هذه الدار، فإن لم تكن الدار ملكاً له فالمنع بالقول، وفي الملك بالقول والفعل، ولو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فاستعار فلان دار جاره واتخذ فيها وليمة ودخلها الحالف لا يحنث ا هـ . فقولهم إن المستعارة تضاف إليه معناه إذا سكنها لا إذا اتخذ فيها وليمة. وفي العدة: لو قال والله لا أدخل هذه الدار وأدخل هذه الدار ، فإذا دخل الأولى يحنث، وإن دخل الثانية لا يحنث. ولو قال والله لا أدخل هذه الدار أو أدخل هذه الدار بنصب اللام، فإن دخل الدار الأولى أولاً ثم دخل الثانية يحنث، وإن دخل الثانية أولاً ثم دخل الأولى لا يحنث لأن كلمة «أو» بمنزله «حتى» ا هـ . وفي مآل الفتاوى قال لا أدخل دار فلان أو دار الفلان لا فرق بينهما عند أبي يوسف، ولو دخل داراً اشتراها بعد اليمين لا يحنث ا هـ . ثم شرع المصنف في الكلام على السكني لأنها تعقب الدخول .
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oath regarding Entering and Leaving. And the middle [slave] is freed by the third statement, and the third is freed by the freeing of the first, because the oath-taker claimed he was lying in all, so freeing all is incumbent upon him. And if he said, 'If I enter Kufa and I have not married, then my slave is free,' then if he entered before marriage, he breaks the oath. And if he said, 'and I have not married' (fa-lam atazawwaj), it means that the marriage is after entering at the time he enters. And if he said, 'If I enter Kufa then I have not married,' it means that he marries after entering forever. End. In al-Qunya: He was in the winter house and argued with his wife and said, 'If I enter this house until Eid, then the lawful is forbidden to me,' then he said, 'I intended that specific house,' he is believed. He swore not to enter upon these people, then he entered the threshold of the door and saw one of them and returned; he does not break the oath. End. In al-Khulasa: He said to his wife, 'If you enter your father's house, then every woman I marry is divorced,' then she entered her father's house, then she became forbidden to him, he married her; she is not divorced by that oath because it is definite by the addition of the oath, so it does not fall under the indefinite; this is in Majmu' al-Nawazil. In al-Nawazil: He said to his wife, 'If you enter the house, then my women are divorced,' she entered the house; divorce falls on her and on others, and reliance is on this, not on what is mentioned in Majmu' al-Nawazil. And if he said to his wife, 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced without loss,' her acceptance at the time of entering the house is a condition, and the interpretation of 'without loss' is that she waives the dower, then she entered the house. End. In al-Umda: If he said, 'I will not let so-and-so enter this house,' if the house is not his property, then prevention is by speech; if it is his property, by speech and action. And if he swore not to enter so-and-so's house, then so-and-so borrowed his neighbor's house and held a wedding feast therein, and the oath-taker entered it, he does not break the oath. End. So their statement that the borrowed house is attributed to him means if he dwells in it, not if he holds a feast in it. In al-Idda: If he said, 'By Allah, I will not enter this house and enter this house,' then if he enters the first, he breaks the oath; if he enters the second, he does not break. And if he said, 'By Allah, I will not enter this house or enter this house' with nasb on the lam, then if he enters the first house first then enters the second, he breaks the oath; if he enters the second first then enters the first, he does not break because the word 'or' serves as 'until'. End. In Ma'al al-Fatawa: He said, 'I will not enter the house of so-and-so or the house of so-and-so,' there is no difference between them according to Abu Yusuf. And if he entered a house he bought after the oath, he does not break the oath. End. Then the author began speaking about residence because it follows entering.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from various Hanafi works concerning oaths related to entering places, often combined with marriage or divorce conditions. The commentator presents these to expand on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. The first ruling deals with a complex case of freeing slaves: if a man makes three statements implying all his slaves are free if he enters, the middle slave is freed by the third statement and the third by the first, since his claim of falsehood applies to all. Then come conditions of entering Kufa with marriage: the conjunction 'wa' or 'thumma' affects the timing of marriage required to avoid hanth. The subsequent rulings from al-Qunya, al-Khulasa, al-Nawazil, al-Umda, and al-Idda provide further nuances: a wife's entry into her father's house may trigger divorce oaths for all women or just specific ones; borrowed houses are attributed to the borrower only for dwelling, not for temporary use like feasts; the particle 'aw' functions like 'hatta' in sequential oaths. Abu Yusuf's view on oaths involving multiple persons is noted. The section concludes with the transition to the topic of residence (sukna) as a natural sequel to entry.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking of an oath, triggering the conditional consequence (e.g., freeing a slave, divorce).
واو الحال (waw al-hal): The conjunctive 'wa' indicating the state of the subject at the time of the main verb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'If I enter Kufa then I have not married, my slave is free.' He enters Kufa and marries a week later. Does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because he married after entry.
B) No, because 'thumma' allows marriage at any time after entry.
C) It depends on his intention.
D) The oath is invalid because the condition is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The temporal conjunction in an oath determines when the condition is evaluated, and ignoring the nuance can lead to incorrect rulings.

Oaths of Entry: Divorce, Ownership, and the Particle 'Aw'
Rulings on divorce oaths, ownership, borrowed houses, and sequential entry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how ownership of a house and the particle 'aw' affect hanth in entry oaths.

Core Idea
Ownership determines whether prevention is by speech or action, and 'aw' can function as 'until' in sequential entry oaths.

This lesson continues the exposition from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, presenting rulings on oaths involving divorce conditional on entry, prevention of entry into owned vs. non-owned houses, and the particle 'aw' serving as 'until'. The distinction between a borrowed house for dwelling versus a temporary event is crucial, as is the interpretation of 'aw' in sequential oaths. These rulings demonstrate the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning in everyday scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the Divorce Oath
Examine the ruling from al-Khulasa: a man says 'If you enter your father's house, every woman I marry is divorced.' The wife enters her father's house, then becomes forbidden to him (e.g., by her own divorce or death of husband? The text says 'she became forbidden to him' but then he marries her again). The ruling: she is not divorced by that oath because the phrase 'every woman' is made definite by the addition of the oath (so it refers to women other than her).
This is a case of 'urf: the oath is understood to apply to future marriages, not the current wife.
2
Examine the Ownership Ruling
From al-Umda: if a man says 'I will not allow so-and-so to enter this house,' if the house is not his property, prevention is by speech only (he can't physically stop him); if it is his property, he can use action.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that ownership grants the right to physically enforce conditions.
3
Understand the Borrowed House Ruling
If a man swears not to enter so-and-so's house, and so-and-so borrows a neighbor's house for a wedding feast, entering that borrowed house does not break the oath because the attribution of the house to the borrower is only for dwelling, not for temporary use.
The key is the nature of possession: borrowing for an event does not make it 'his house' in the legal sense.
4
Interpret the Particle 'Aw'
From al-Idda: if a man says 'I will not enter this house or enter this house' with nasb, the particle 'aw' functions like 'hatta' (until). If he enters the second house first and then the first, he does not break the oath because he enters the first after the second, which is like 'until I enter the second'.
This is a unique Hanafi interpretation: 'aw' can indicate sequential order in oaths.
