كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج والوسط عتق بالكلام الثالث وعتق الثالث بعتق الأول، لأن الحالف زعم أنه كاذب في الكل فيلزمه عتق الكل. ولو قال إن دخلت الكوفة ولم أتزوج فعبدي حر، فإن دخل قبل التزوج حنث، ولو قال فلم أتزوج فهذا على أن يكون التزوج بعد الدخول حين يدخل. ولو قال إن دخلت الكوفة ثم لم أتزوج فهو على أن يتزوج بعد الدخول على الأبد ا هـ. وفي القنية : كان في البيت الشتوي فخاصم امرأته فقال إن دخلت هذا البيت إلى العيد فالحلال عليه حرام، ثم قال نويت ذلك البيت بعينه يصدق. حلف لا يدخل على هؤلاء القوم ثم دخل عتبة الباب فرأى واحداً منهم فرجع لا يحنث ا هـ. وفي الخلاصة: قال لامرأته إن دخلت دار أبيك فكل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق فدخل دار أبيها ثم إنها حرمت عليه فتزوجها لا تطلق بتلك اليمين لأنها معرفة بإضافة اليمين فلا تدخل تحت النكرة؛ هذا في مجموع النوازل. وفي النوازل : قال لامرأته إن دخلت الدار فنسائي طوالق فدخلت الدار وقع الطلاق عليها وعلى غيرها والاعتماد على هذا دون ما ذكر في مجموع النوازل، ولو قال لامرأته إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق بغير خسران يشترط قبولها عند دخول الدار وتفسير غير الخسران إن وهبت المهر ثم دخلت الدار ا.هـ. وفي العمدة لو قال لا أدع فلاناً يدخل هذه الدار، فإن لم تكن الدار ملكاً له فالمنع بالقول، وفي الملك بالقول والفعل، ولو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان فاستعار فلان دار جاره واتخذ فيها وليمة ودخلها الحالف لا يحنث ا هـ . فقولهم إن المستعارة تضاف إليه معناه إذا سكنها لا إذا اتخذ فيها وليمة. وفي العدة: لو قال والله لا أدخل هذه الدار وأدخل هذه الدار ، فإذا دخل الأولى يحنث، وإن دخل الثانية لا يحنث. ولو قال والله لا أدخل هذه الدار أو أدخل هذه الدار بنصب اللام، فإن دخل الدار الأولى أولاً ثم دخل الثانية يحنث، وإن دخل الثانية أولاً ثم دخل الأولى لا يحنث لأن كلمة «أو» بمنزله «حتى» ا هـ . وفي مآل الفتاوى قال لا أدخل دار فلان أو دار الفلان لا فرق بينهما عند أبي يوسف، ولو دخل داراً اشتراها بعد اليمين لا يحنث ا هـ . ثم شرع المصنف في الكلام على السكني لأنها تعقب الدخول .
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oath regarding Entering and Leaving. And the middle [slave] is freed by the third statement, and the third is freed by the freeing of the first, because the oath-taker claimed he was lying in all, so freeing all is incumbent upon him. And if he said, 'If I enter Kufa and I have not married, then my slave is free,' then if he entered before marriage, he breaks the oath. And if he said, 'and I have not married' (fa-lam atazawwaj), it means that the marriage is after entering at the time he enters. And if he said, 'If I enter Kufa then I have not married,' it means that he marries after entering forever. End. In al-Qunya: He was in the winter house and argued with his wife and said, 'If I enter this house until Eid, then the lawful is forbidden to me,' then he said, 'I intended that specific house,' he is believed. He swore not to enter upon these people, then he entered the threshold of the door and saw one of them and returned; he does not break the oath. End. In al-Khulasa: He said to his wife, 'If you enter your father's house, then every woman I marry is divorced,' then she entered her father's house, then she became forbidden to him, he married her; she is not divorced by that oath because it is definite by the addition of the oath, so it does not fall under the indefinite; this is in Majmu' al-Nawazil. In al-Nawazil: He said to his wife, 'If you enter the house, then my women are divorced,' she entered the house; divorce falls on her and on others, and reliance is on this, not on what is mentioned in Majmu' al-Nawazil. And if he said to his wife, 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced without loss,' her acceptance at the time of entering the house is a condition, and the interpretation of 'without loss' is that she waives the dower, then she entered the house. End. In al-Umda: If he said, 'I will not let so-and-so enter this house,' if the house is not his property, then prevention is by speech; if it is his property, by speech and action. And if he swore not to enter so-and-so's house, then so-and-so borrowed his neighbor's house and held a wedding feast therein, and the oath-taker entered it, he does not break the oath. End. So their statement that the borrowed house is attributed to him means if he dwells in it, not if he holds a feast in it. In al-Idda: If he said, 'By Allah, I will not enter this house and enter this house,' then if he enters the first, he breaks the oath; if he enters the second, he does not break. And if he said, 'By Allah, I will not enter this house or enter this house' with nasb on the lam, then if he enters the first house first then enters the second, he breaks the oath; if he enters the second first then enters the first, he does not break because the word 'or' serves as 'until'. End. In Ma'al al-Fatawa: He said, 'I will not enter the house of so-and-so or the house of so-and-so,' there is no difference between them according to Abu Yusuf. And if he entered a house he bought after the oath, he does not break the oath. End. Then the author began speaking about residence because it follows entering.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from various Hanafi works concerning oaths related to entering places, often combined with marriage or divorce conditions. The commentator presents these to expand on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. The first ruling deals with a complex case of freeing slaves: if a man makes three statements implying all his slaves are free if he enters, the middle slave is freed by the third statement and the third by the first, since his claim of falsehood applies to all. Then come conditions of entering Kufa with marriage: the conjunction 'wa' or 'thumma' affects the timing of marriage required to avoid hanth. The subsequent rulings from al-Qunya, al-Khulasa, al-Nawazil, al-Umda, and al-Idda provide further nuances: a wife's entry into her father's house may trigger divorce oaths for all women or just specific ones; borrowed houses are attributed to the borrower only for dwelling, not for temporary use like feasts; the particle 'aw' functions like 'hatta' in sequential oaths. Abu Yusuf's view on oaths involving multiple persons is noted. The section concludes with the transition to the topic of residence (sukna) as a natural sequel to entry.

Key Terms
المستعارة (al-musta'ara): A borrowed house; legal attribution is limited to dwelling, not temporary events.
أو (aw): The particle 'or', which in sequential oaths functions as 'until' according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says 'By Allah, I will not enter this house or enter this house' (with nasb). He enters the second house first, then the first. Does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because he entered both houses.
B) No, because the particle 'or' means 'until', so entering the second first and then the first is not a breach.
C) Yes, because he entered the first house at any time.
D) No, because he intended to enter both houses.

Key Takeaway
Ownership and the particle 'aw' significantly impact hanth in entry oaths; careful analysis of context and wording is essential.

Talaq — Divorce

Defining Khuruj (Exit) and Its Legal Application in Oaths and Divorce
Defining exit and providing rules for oaths and divorce conditional upon exiting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khuruj (exit) as per Hanafi fiqh and apply its definition to determine whether an oath is broken or a conditional divorce occurs in various scenarios.

Core Idea
The Hanafi definition of khuruj depends on context: exit from a house requires moving family and goods; exit from a city requires only the body.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the legal meaning of 'exit' (khuruj) as it appears in oaths and conditional divorce. The definition is not uniform; it varies based on the location and the intent of the oath-taker. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly determining when an oath is broken or a divorce takes effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its legal significance
Understand that khuruj is a key term in oaths and conditional divorce, and its definition determines whether legal consequences (hanath or talaq) occur.
2
Distinguish between exit from a house vs. a city
Note that for houses, exit includes moving oneself, belongings, and family if the oath is about residence; for cities, only the body leaves.
3
Apply the definition to specific scenarios
Work through examples: stepping into the courtyard, exiting through different doors, and the effect of exceptions (istithna).
4
Recognize the role of intention and exceptions
Understand when intention matters (e.g., if he intends to exit the city, but the oath did not specify) and how exceptions prevent oath-breaking.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi discussion on the masjid example
Examine whether the example of a masjid is a restriction or just an illustration; note the differing views and the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخرج إلا إلى جنازة فخرج إليها ثم أتى حاجة لا يخرج أو لا يذهب إلى مكة فخرج إمساكها فأدخلته خلاف ا هـ . وفي البدائع : الخروج هو الانفصال من الحصن إلى العورة على مضادة الدخول فلا يكون المكث بعد الخروج خروجاً كما لا يكون المكث بعد الدخول دخولاً، ثم الخروج كما يكون في البلدان والدور والمنازل والبيوت تكون من الأخبية والفساطيط والخيم والسفن لوجود حده، والخروج من الدور المسكونة أن يخرج الحالف بنفسه ومتاعه وعياله كما إذا حلف لا يسكن، والخروج من البلدان والقرى أن يخرج الحالف ببدنه خاصة. ولو قال والله لا أخرج وهو في بيت من الدار فخرج إلى صحن الدار لم يحنث إلا أن ينوي، فإن نوى الخروج إلى مكة أو خروجاً من البلد لم يصدق قضاء ولا ديانة لأن غير المذكور لا يحتمل التخصيص. ولو قال إن خرجت من هذه الدار فأنت طالق فخرجت منها من الباب أي باب كان ومن أي موضع كان من فوق حائط أو سطح أو نقب حنث لوجود الشرط وهو الخروج من الدار ، ولو قيد بباب هذه الدار لم يحنث بالخروج من غير الباب قديماً كان الباب أو حادثاً، ولو عين باباً في اليمين تعين ولا يحنث بالخروج من غيره
هذه
قوله : (كلا يخرج إلا إلى جنازة فخرج إليها ثم أتى حاجة يعني لا يحنث لأن الموجود خروج مستثنى والمضي بعد ذلك ليس بخروج. وفي البدائع : لو قال إن خرجت من الدار إلا إلى المسجد فأنت طالق فخرجت تريد المسجد ثم بدا لها فذهبت إلى غير المسجد لم تطلق لما ذكرنا . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال إن خرجت من هذه الدار فلان فأنت طالق مع فخرجت وحدها أو مع فلان آخر ثم خرج فلان ولحقها فإنه لا يحنث لأن كلمة «مع» للقران فيقتضي مقارنتها للخروج ولم يوجد لأن المكث بعد الخروج ليس بخروج كما في البدائع أيضاً : ولو خرج في مسألة الكتاب لغير الجنازة فإنه يحنث لوجود الشرط والاعتبار للقصد
في قوله لا أسكن شرط الحنث السكنى والفعل لا يتحقق بدون الاختيار. وفي قوله «إن لم أخرج» شرط الحنث عدم الخروج العدم يتحقق بدون الاختيار انتهت فتأمل قوله: (والخروج من الدور المسكونة أن يخرج الحالف بنفسه ومتاعه وعياله عزاه في الذخيرة والتتارخانية إلى القدوري، وقد قيد في النهر مسألة المتن بقوله حلف لا يخرج من هذا المسجد مثلاً فاخرج محمولاً الخ» ثم نقل عبارة البدائع هذه المسألة قال : وعلى هذا فمن صور في البيت يحمل كلامه على أن الحالف كان تبعاً لغيره في السكنى كما مر اهـ . قلت : وقد وقع تقييد المسألة أيضاً بالمسجد في كلام الإمام محمد في الجامع الصغير لكن قال في غاية البيان: إنه ليس بقيد .اهـ. ويدل عليه ما في الخانية والظهيرية : رجل قال والله لا أخرج من بلد كذا فهو على أن يخرج ببدنه ولو قال لا أخرج من هذه الدار فهو على النقلة منها بأهله إن كان ساكناً فيها ! دل الدليل على أنه أراد به الخروج ببدنه اهـ. فمن صور المسألة بالبيت مراده حيث دل الدليل على أن المراد الخروج ببدنه لكن التصوير بالمسجد كما فعل
He does not exit except to a funeral, then he exits to it, then he attends to a need: he does not break his oath. Or he does not go to Mecca, then he exits under his wife's control? She brought him in? Disagreement. End. In al-Bada'i: Al-khuruj (exit) is the separation from a fortress to an open area as opposed to entry, so remaining after exit is not exit, just as remaining after entry is not entry. Then exit, as it is in cities, houses, dwellings, houses, is also from tents, pavilions, tents, and ships due to their having a boundary. And exit from inhabited houses is that the oath-taker exits with himself, his belongings, and his family, as if he swears not to reside. And exit from cities and villages is that the oath-taker exits with his body only. And if he says, 'By Allah, I will not exit' while he is in a room of the house, and he exits to the courtyard of the house, he does not break his oath unless he intends [otherwise]. If he intends to exit to Mecca or exit from the city, he is not believed judicially nor religiously because what is not mentioned does not admit specification. And if he says, 'If you exit from this house, you are divorced,' and she exits from it through the door, any door, and from any place, from above the wall, roof, or hole, he breaks his oath due to existence of the condition which is exit from the house. And if he restricted it to a specific door of this house, he does not break his oath by exiting from another door, whether the door was old or new. And if he specified a door in the oath, it is specific, and he does not break his oath by exiting from other than it. This.
His statement: ('He does not exit except to a funeral, then he exits to it, then he attends to a need') means he does not break his oath because what occurred is an excepted exit, and moving after that is not exit. And in al-Bada'i: If he says, 'If you exit from the house except to the mosque, you are divorced,' and she exits intending the mosque, then she changed and went to other than the mosque, she is not divorced for what we mentioned. And the author indicated that if he says, 'If you exit from this house with Zayd, you are divorced,' and she exits alone or with another Zayd? then Zayd exits and catches up with her: he does not break his oath because the word 'with' indicates simultaneity, requiring her exit to be simultaneous with his, and this did not occur because staying after exit is not exit, as in al-Bada'i also: and if he exits in the issue of the book for other than a funeral, he breaks his oath because of existence of the condition, and consideration is for intent.
In his statement 'I will not reside,' the condition for breaking the oath is residence, and an action cannot be realized without choice. In his statement 'If I do not exit,' the condition for breaking the oath is non-exit, and non-existence can be realized without choice. End. So reflect on his statement: ('And exit from inhabited houses is that the oath-taker exits with himself, his belongings, and his family') attributed in al-Dhakhira and al-Tatarkhaniyya to al-Quduri. And in al-Nahr, the issue of the matn is restricted by his saying 'he swore not to exit from this masjid, for example, then he was carried out...' Then he quotes the wording of al-Bada'i on this issue, saying: On this, if one pictures the issue in a house, his speech is interpreted that the oath-taker was a dependent of others in residence, as passed. End. I say: The issue has also been restricted by the masjid in the speech of Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir, but he said in Ghayat al-Bayan that it is not a restriction. End. And it is indicated by what is in al-Khaniyya and al-Zahira: A man said, 'By Allah, I will not exit from such-and-such a city,' then it is about him exiting with his body. And if he said, 'I will not exit from this house,' then it is about moving from it with his family if he is its resident! The evidence indicates that he intended exit with his body. End. So whoever pictures the issue with a house, his intention is where the evidence indicates that the intended meaning is exit with his body, but the picturing with the masjid is as he did.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and applicatory commentary on the concept of khuruj (exit) in the context of oaths. It begins by addressing a specific oath: 'he does not exit except to a funeral'—if he exits for the funeral then afterwards attends to another need, he does not break his oath because the exit was excused and afterward is not considered a new exit. The passage then provides a comprehensive definition of khuruj from al-Bada'i: exit is the separation from an enclosed area to an open area, as opposed to entry. Importantly, staying after exit is not exit, just as staying after entry is not entry. The definition applies to cities, houses, tents, etc. when they have a defined boundary. For inhabited houses, exit requires moving oneself, belongings, and family if the oath is about residence; for cities and villages, exit is with one's body only. If someone swears not to exit while in a room and steps into the courtyard, he does not break his oath unless he intends a full departure from the house. His intention to exit to Mecca or from the city is not accepted in court or between him and Allah because the oath did not specify that. The passage then gives examples of conditional divorce: if the condition is 'if you exit from this house,' exiting from any part (door, wall, roof) breaks the condition. But if the door is specified, exiting from another door does not. If the condition includes an exception (e.g., except to the mosque), going to the mosque then changing course does not trigger divorce. Similarly, if the condition is 'if you exit with Zayd,' and she exits alone then Zayd catches up, that does not count as simultaneous exit. The passage ends with a discussion of the interpretation of 'exit' in different contexts, quoting various authorities like al-Quduri, al-Dhakhira, etc., and noting that picturing the issue with a masjid is not a restriction; the general principle is that the meaning of exit depends on the subject matter and evidence.

Key Terms
الخروج (al-khuruj): Physical separation from an enclosed area (like a fortress, house, city) to an open area, in opposition to entry.
الحنث (al-hanath): Breaking of an oath, requiring kaffarah.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): An exception in an oath that allows the prohibited action under specific conditions without breaking the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not exit from this house.' He steps into the courtyard of the same house. According to the Hanafi definition from al-Bada'i, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he left the room.
B) No, because the courtyard is still part of the house.
C) Yes, if he intended to leave the house.
D) No, unless he exits the entire house boundary.

Key Takeaway
The legal meaning of khuruj in oaths is determined by context and custom, not mere physical movement.

Oaths Conditional on Permission: Duration and Validity
Rulings on oaths conditioned on permission and their validity after permission ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath conditioned on permission is restricted to the duration of the right to give permission, and distinguish cases where the oath persists or breaks.

Core Idea
An oath conditioned on permission applies only as long as the right to grant permission exists.

When a person swears an oath that includes an exception for permission (e.g., 'except with my permission'), the Hanafi school holds that the oath is limited to the period during which the person granting permission has the right to prevent the action. This right may be based on marriage, ownership, or authority. Once that right ends, the oath no longer applies, even if the same relationship is later restored. This lesson unpacks this principle through various examples and clarifies when an oath is broken (hanth) and when it is nullified entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Look for phrases like 'except with my permission' or 'except by the order of so-and-so'. These indicate the oath is conditional on permission.
The condition must be explicitly stated; general oaths without such a clause are treated differently.
2
Determine the right to permission
Determine who has the right to prevent the action—typically a husband over his wife's movements, a master over his slave, or a creditor over the debtor's exit from the house of demand.
This right must exist at the time of the oath for the condition to be meaningful.
3
Apply the duration limit
The oath is only binding while the right to permission exists. If the right ends (e.g., irrevocable divorce, sale of slave, death of permittor), the oath falls.
If the right is later restored (e.g., remarriage), the oath does not revive; a new oath would be needed.
4
Check for exceptions and disputes
Consider cases where the permission becomes impossible (e.g., permittor goes mad or dies). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the oath is nullified; according to Abu Hanifa, it persists.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) may vary; modern practice often follows the view that the oath falls.
5
Distinguish court vs. conscience
In court, the literal wording is applied; but between the person and Allah, intention matters. For example, if someone intended 'as long as she is my wife', they are truthful in conscience but the court follows the general wording.
This distinction protects individuals from being penalized for private intentions.

Source Text

كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج

العبد فقال إن ضربت تقید به کاجلس فتغد عندي فقال إن تغديت ومركب عبده مركبه إن ينو ولا دين . . وإلى أنه لو قال عبدي حر إن دخلت هذه الدار دخلة إلا أن يأمرني فلان فأمره فلان مرة واحدة فإنه لا يحنث وقد سقطت اليمين بخلاف ما إذا قال إلا أن يأمرني بها فلان بزيادة بها فأمره فدخل ثم دخل بعد ذلك بغير أمره فإنه يحنث . ولا بد من الأمر في كل دخلة كقوله إلا بأمر فلان كالمسألة الأولى كما في البدائع أيضاً. وفي الظهيرية. قال لامرأته إن دخلت من هذه الدار إلا لأمر لا بد منه فأنت طالق وللمرأة حق على رجل فأرادت أن ذلك تدعي وخرجت لأجله قالوا: إن كانت تقدر على أن توكل بذلك حنث الحالف، وإن لم تقدر على أن توكل لا يحنث ولو حلف أن لا تخرج امرأته إلا بعلمه فخرجت وهو يراها فمنعها لم يحنث، ولو أذن لها بالخروج فخرجت بغير علمه لا يحنث، وإن لم يأذن لها فخرجت وهو يراها لا يحنث أيضاً .اهـ. ثم انعقاد اليمين على الإذن في قوله إن خرجت إلا بإذني فأنت طالق أو والله لا تخرجين إلا بإذني مقيد ببقاء النكاح لأن الإذن إنما يصح ممن له المنع ، فلو أبانها ثم . تزوجها فخرجت بلا إذن لم يحنث وإن كان زوال الملك لا يبطل اليمين عندنا لأنها لم تنعقد إلا على مدة بقاء النكاح وكذا في العبد يشترط بقاء ملك المولى وسيأتي بيان أيضاً في قوله حلف ليعلمنه بكل داعر دخل البلدة تقيد بقيام ولايته، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا تخرج امرأته هذه الدار ولا عبده فبانت منه أو خرج العبد عن ملكه ثم خرج فإنه يحنث، ولا يتقيد بحال قيام الزوجية والملك لانعدام دلالة التقييد وهي قوله إلا بإذنه فيعمل بعموم اللفظ، فإن عنی به ما دامت امرأته دين فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى، ولا يدين في القضاء لأنه خلاف الظاهر. وكذلك من طولب بحق فحلف أن لا يخرج من دار مطالبه حنث بالخروج زال ذلك الحق أو لم يزل لما قلنا ؛ كذا في البدائع. وفي المحيط : رجل حلفه ثلاثة رجال أنه لا يخرج من بخارى إلا بإذنهم فجن أحدهم قال لا يخرج وإن مات أحد الثلاثة فخرج لم يحنث لأنه ذهب الإذن الذي وقعت عليه اليمين ولو قال إلا بإذن فلان فمات المحلوف عليه بطلت اليمين عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف بناء على أن فوات المعقود عليه يمنع بقاء اليمين عندهما،

وعنده لا
اهـ. يمنع
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oath Regarding Entering and Exiting. [Page 529] The slave: if he said 'If I beat you' it is restricted like 'Sit and eat lunch with me' and he said 'If I eat lunch' ... And also: if he said 'My slave is free if I enter this house once except if so-and-so orders me' and then so-and-so ordered him once, then he does not break his oath and the oath is dropped. Contrary to if he said 'except if so-and-so orders me to it' with the addition of 'to it', and he ordered him, so he entered, then entered again without his order; then he breaks his oath. And permission is required for each entry, like his saying 'except by the order of so-and-so' as in the first issue, as also in al-Bada'i'. And in al-Zahiriyya: He said to his wife 'If you enter this house except for an unavoidable matter, you are divorced' and the woman had a right against a man, so she wanted to claim it and went out for it. They said: If she could appoint an agent for that, the oath-taker breaks his oath; if she could not appoint an agent, he does not break his oath. And if he swore that his wife would not go out except with his knowledge, and she went out while he saw her and he prevented her, he does not break his oath. And if he permitted her to go out and she went out without his knowledge, he does not break his oath. And if he did not permit her and she went out while he saw her, he also does not break his oath — end. Then, the binding of the oath on permission in his saying 'If you go out except with my permission, you are divorced' or 'By Allah, you must not go out except with my permission' is restricted to the continuation of marriage, because permission is only valid from the one who has the right to prevent. So if he divorced her irrevocably (bā'inan) then married her again and she went out without permission, he does not break his oath, even though loss of ownership does not nullify the oath in our view, because the oath was only contracted for the duration of the marriage. Similarly, for the slave, continuation of the master's ownership is required. And an explanation will also come in his saying 'He swore to inform about every corrupt person entering the town' — it is restricted by the continuation of his authority. This is contrary to if he swore that his wife would not go out of this house, or his slave, and then she was separated from him or the slave left his ownership, and then they went out: he does break his oath, and it is not restricted to the state of marriage and ownership, because there is no indication of restriction, namely his saying 'except with his permission'; so the general wording is applied. If he intended 'as long as she is his wife', he is truthful between himself and Allah, but not in the court because it is contrary to the apparent meaning. Similarly, one who is demanded a right and swears that he will not leave the house of his demand, he breaks his oath whether the right is removed or not, for what we said — as in al-Bada'i'. And in al-Muhit: A man was made to swear by three men that he would not leave Bukhara except with their permission. One of them went mad: they said he must not leave. If one of the three died and he left, he does not break his oath because the permission on which the oath was based has disappeared. And if he said 'except with the permission of so-and-so' and the one sworn by died, the oath is nullified according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf (?) based on the fact that the loss of the subject matter prevents the continuation of the oath according to them, while according to him it does not prevent — end. He said 'it does not prevent'.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a collection of rulings on oaths that are conditioned on permission (إذن). The author, Ibn Nujaym, expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The key principle is that when an oath is phrased with an exception for permission (e.g., 'except if so-and-so orders me' or 'except with my permission'), the oath is restricted to the duration of the right of permission. For example, if a man swears that his wife will not go out except with his permission, the oath only applies as long as she is his wife. If he divorces her irrevocably and then remarries her, the oath is not revived; she can go out without permission without breaking the oath. Similarly, for a slave, the oath applies only while the slave is owned. However, if the oath is not phrased with an exception for permission but simply states 'my wife will not go out of this house', then if she is divorced and later goes out, the oath is broken because there is no indication of restriction. The passage also covers cases where permission cannot be given (e.g., the permittor goes mad or dies) and the effect on the oath: if the permission is impossible, the oath becomes void. The ruling distinguishes between the court's judgment (which follows the literal wording) and the person's conscience (where intention matters).

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission given by the one who has the right to prevent an action; in oaths, it conditions the oath on the existence of permission.
حنث (hanith): Breaking an oath by doing the thing sworn against; triggers the need for expiation (kaffarah) unless the oath is voided.
يمين معلقة (yamin mu'allaqah): A suspended oath, where the fulfillment of the oath is conditional on a future event; here, conditioned on the absence of permission.
بقاء النكاح (baqa' al-nikah): Continuation of marriage; the period during which the marriage bond exists and the husband has the right to restrict his wife's movements.
المعقود عليه (al-ma'qud 'alayhi): The subject matter of the oath; here, the permittor whose permission is the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears: 'By Allah, my wife will not go out except with my permission.' He then divorces her irrevocably (bā'in) and later remarries her. She goes out without permission. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the oath was made and the marriage resumed.
B) No, because the oath was restricted to the duration of the first marriage; after irrevocable divorce, the right to permission ended.
C) Yes, because the wife's action occurred after remarriage, so the oath applies anew.
D) No, because the wife is not bound by any oath after divorce.

Key Takeaway
An oath conditioned on permission is limited to the duration of the right to grant that permission; when the right ends, the oath falls and does not revive.

The Oath of Immediacy (Yamin al-Fawr)
Definition and examples of the oath of immediacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the oath of immediacy according to Abu Hanifa, apply it to scenarios involving imminent actions, and explain the dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
An oath of immediacy is permanent in wording but temporary in meaning, restricted to the immediate context by the situation.

This lesson explores a unique Hanafi concept: the oath of immediacy (yamin al-fawr). Imam Abu Hanifa introduced this category to capture oaths that, though expressed in absolute terms, are understood to apply only to the immediate action at hand. Through concrete examples and a comparison with other schools, you will learn when such an oath is broken and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept
Recognize that an oath of immediacy is an oath whose wording is absolute but whose scope is restricted to the immediate situation by a contextual cue (qarina).
The term 'fawr' originally means boiling, then speed.
2
Distinguish from absolute oaths
Contrast with perpetual oaths (absolute) and temporary oaths (time-bound). Note that Abu Hanifa added a third category.
3
Apply the rule to examples
In scenarios (woman about to go out, slave about to be beaten, invitation to lunch), determine that the oath applies only to the imminent action. If the action is delayed, no breaking.
4
Analyze the dispute
Understand that Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold the oath is absolute because the wording is unqualified. Evaluate the Hanafi response based on contextual indication.
The dispute is about the weight of contextual cues vs. literal wording.
5
Recognize explicit vs. implicit restriction
Note that restriction can be explicit (e.g., adding 'today') or implicit via verbal or situational cues. Verbal implication includes swearing not to enter upon someone (restricted to his lifetime).
Situational implication is the core of yamin al-fawr.

قوله : (ولو أرادت الخروج فقال إن خرجت أو ضرب العبد فقال أن ضربت تقيد به كاجلس فتغد عندي فقال إن تغديت بيان ليمين الفور مأخوذ من فور القدر إذا غلت، واستعير للسرعة ثم سميت بها الحال التي لا ريث فيها فقيل جاء فلان من فوره أي من ساعته. وسميت هذه اليمين به باعتبار فوران الغضب انفرد أبو حنيفة بإظهارها وكانت اليمين في عرفهم قسمين : مؤبدة وهي أن يحلف مطلقاً، ومؤقته وهي أن يحلف أن لا يفعل كذا اليوم أو هذا الشهر فأخرج أبو حنيفة يمين الفور. قال في المحيط : ولم يسبقه أحد في تسميتها ولا في حكمها ولا خالفه أحد فيه بعد ذلك فإن الناس كلهم عيال أبي حنيفة في هذا اهـ. بل الناس عيال أبي حنيفة في الفقه كله وهي يمين مؤبدة لفظاً مؤقتة معنى تتقيد بالحال أو تكون بناء على أمر حالي فمن الثاني امرأة تهيأت للخروج فحلف لا تخرج فإذا جلست ساعة ثم خرجت لا يحنث لأن قصده أن يمنعها من الخروج الذي تهيأت له فكأنه قال إن خرجت أي الساعة. ومنه من أراد أن يضرب عبده فحلف عليه لا يضربه فإذا تركه ساعة بحيث يذهب فور ذلك ثم ضربه لا يحنث لذلك بعينه. ومن الأول اجلس فتغد عندي فيقول إن تغديت فعبدي حر تقيد بالحال فإذا تغدى في يومه في منزله لا يحنث لأنه يمين وقع جواباً تضمن إعادة ما في السؤال والمسؤول الغد الحالي فينصرف الحلف إلى الغداء الحالي لتقع المطابقة، وهذا كله عند عدم نية الحالف . وقيد بكونه قال إن تغديت ولم يزد عليه لأنه لو زاد بأن قال إن تغديت اليوم أو معك فعبدي حر فتغدى في بيته أو في وقت آخر فإنه يحنث لأنه زاد على حرف الجواب فيكون مبتدأ أو لا يقال إن موسى عليه السلام زاد في الجواب حين سئل عن العصا ولم يكن مبتدأ لأنا نقول : لما سئل بما وهي تقع على ذات ما لا يعقل والصفات فاشتبه عليه الحال فأجاب بهما حتى يكون مجيباً عن أيهما كان. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال لامرأته عند خروجها من المنزل إن رجعت إلى منزلي فأنت طالق ثلاثاً ثم جلست فلم تخرج زماناً ثم خرجت ورجعت والرجل يقول نويت الفور فالظاهر أنه يصدق لأنه لو قال إن خرجت ولا نية له ينصرف إلى هذه الخرجة فكذا إذا قال إن رجعت ونوى الرجوع بعد هذه الخرجة كان أولى أن ينصرف إلى الرجوع عن هذه الخرجة؛ كذا في المحيط . ثم اعلم أن التقييد تارة يثبت صريحاً وتارة يثبت دلالة والدلالة نوعان : دلالة لفظية ودلاة حالية، فدلالة اللفظ نحو ما إذا حلف لا يدخل على فلان تقيد بحال حياة المحلوف عليه، والدلالة الحالية كما في الكتاب وفي المحيط : أصله أن الحالف أعقب الفعل فعلاً متی بحرف العطف وهو الفاء والواو فإن كان الفعل الثاني في العادة يفعل على فور الأول ولم يفعل حنث، وإن لم يكن يفعل على فور الأول لا يحنث ما لم يمت، وإن ذكر الفعل الثاني بحرف الشرط أو التراخي وهو حرف ثم فهو على الأبد لأن المشروط لا يتحقق إلا بعد وجود الشرط وكلمة «ثم على التراخي فلو قال إن ضربتني فلم أضربك أو لقيتك فلم أسلم عليك وإن كلمتني فلم أجبك فهو على الفور باعتبار العادة وكذا لو قال إن استعرت قوله : (ولا خالفه أحد فيه بعد ذلك ينافي هذا الإطلاق ما في فتح القدير حيث قال وقال زفر : يحنث وهو قول الشافعي لأنه عقد يمينه على كل غد أو خروج وضرب فاعتبر الإطلاق اللفظي قوله : (فمن الثاني امرأة تهيأت للخروج الخ) قال الشرنبلالية في الفتح ما يشير إلى عدم اشتراط تغير تلك الهيئة الحاصلة مع إرادة الخروج حيث قال : امرأة تهيأت إلى آخر هذه العبارة المذكورة هنا
His saying: (And if she wanted to go out and he said 'If you go out' or he beat the slave and he said 'If I beat you' it is restricted—like 'Sit and eat lunch with me', and he said 'If I eat lunch') is an explanation of the oath of immediacy (yamin al-fawr). It is derived from 'fawr' of a pot when it boils, and was borrowed for speed. Then the state in which there is no delay was named by it, so it was said 'he came from his fawr' i.e., from his very moment. This oath was named by it considering the boiling of anger. Abu Hanifa alone brought it to light. Oaths among them were of two types: perpetual—that he swears absolutely, and temporary—that he swears not to do such-and-such today or this month. So Abu Hanifa extracted the oath of immediacy. He said in al-Muhit: No one preceded him in naming it nor in its ruling, and no one opposed him in that after that, for all people are dependents of Abu Hanifa in this—end. Rather, all people are dependents of Abu Hanifa in all fiqh. It is an oath permanent in wording, temporary in meaning; it is restricted to the situation or based on a situational matter. From the second type: a woman prepares to go out, and he swears she will not go out; if she sits for an hour then goes out, he does not break his oath, because his intent is to prevent the specific going out she prepared for, as if he said 'if you go out now'. From it: a man intends to beat his slave and swears he will not beat him; if he leaves him for a while such that the immediacy passes, then beats him, he does not break his oath for the same reason. From the first type: 'Sit and eat lunch with me' and he says 'If I eat lunch, my slave is free'; it is restricted to the situation. So if he eats lunch on that day in his home, he does not break his oath, because the oath occurred as a response, including a repetition of what was in the question, and what was asked about is the immediate lunch, so the oath is directed to the immediate lunch for correspondence. All this is when the oath-taker has no intention. And it is restricted to his saying 'If I eat lunch' without adding anything; because if he added by saying 'If I eat lunch today' or 'with you', then his slave is free, and he eats in his home or at another time, then he does break his oath, because he added to the letter of the response, so he becomes an initiator. One should not say that Moses (peace be upon him) added to the response when asked about the staff and was not an initiator. We say: when he was asked with 'ma' which falls on the essence of irrational things and attributes, the situation was ambiguous to him, so he answered with both so that he would be answering whichever it was. And the author indicated that if he said to his wife when she was leaving the house, 'If you return to my house, you are divorced with three' and then she sat and did not go out for a time, then went out and returned, and the man says 'I intended immediacy', the apparent is that he is truthful, because if he said 'If you go out' with no intention, it refers to this very going out. So likewise if he said 'If you return' and intends return after this particular going out, it is more appropriate that it refers to return from this particular going out—as in al-Muhit. Then know that restriction is sometimes established explicitly and sometimes implicitly. Implication is of two types: verbal implication and situational implication. Verbal implication is like when he swears he will not enter upon so-and-so, it is restricted to the lifetime of the one sworn about. Situational implication is as in the book. And in al-Muhit: Its basis is that the oath-taker follows the action with an action connected by a conjunction, whether fa or waw. If the second action is customarily done immediately after the first and he does not do it, he breaks his oath. If it is not customarily done immediately after the first, he does not break his oath unless he dies. And if he mentions the second action with a conditional or delaying particle, which is 'thumma', then it is perpetual, because the conditioned is not realized except after the existence of the condition, and the word 'thumma' indicates delay. So if he says 'If you hit me, then I do not hit you' or 'if I meet you, then I do not greet you' or 'if you speak to me, then I do not answer you', it is immediate by custom. Similarly, if he said 'If I borrow...' His saying: (And none opposed him in that after this) contradicts this absolute statement what is in Fath al-Qadir where he said: Zufar said: he breaks his oath, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, because he contracted his oath on every lunch or going out or beating, so he considered the literal absolute wording. His saying: (From the second, a woman prepares to go out...) Al-Shurnaballali said in al-Fath something indicating lack of requirement for the change of that state that occurred with the intention to go out, where he said: 'a woman prepares...' to the end of this statement mentioned here.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq introduces the concept of 'Yamin al-Fawr' (the oath of immediacy), a unique Hanafi contribution attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. The term 'fawr' originally means boiling, then speed; here, it refers to an oath that is permanent in wording but temporary in meaning, restricted to the immediate situation. The author explains that oaths are of two types: perpetual (absolute) and temporary (time-bound). Abu Hanifa extracted a third type: the oath of immediacy, where the oath is understood to apply only to the immediate action. For example, if a woman is about to go out and her husband swears 'If you go out (now), you are divorced', then if she delays and goes out later, the oath is not broken because the husband's intent was to prevent the immediate going out. Similarly, if a man invites you to lunch and you swear 'If I eat lunch (now), my slave is free', it refers to that immediate lunch. The passage also discusses how the addition of words like 'today' or 'with you' makes the oath general, not immediate. A subtle point is made about Prophet Moses' response in the Quran, where he added details without being an initiator. The passage concludes with a discussion of explicit vs. implicit restriction, and a note that Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagreed with Abu Hanifa, considering the literal wording instead. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.

Key Terms
يمين الفور (yamin al-fawr): An oath that is permanent in wording but temporary in meaning, restricted to the immediate situation due to a contextual cue.
دلالة حالية (dalala haliyya): Contextual indication derived from the situation, such as the imminent act of going out or eating, which restricts the oath's application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man sees his slave about to be beaten and swears: 'If you beat him, you are free.' He then delays for an hour and beats him. Does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the literal wording applies to any beating.
B) No, because the oath was an oath of immediacy, applying only to the immediate beating.
C) Yes, because the slave is already free by the oath.
D) No, because beating is not sufficient to free a slave.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely recognizes the oath of immediacy, restricting absolute wording by contextual cues, a concept attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Obligatory Prayer and Fawr in Oaths
Ruling on fawr in conditional divorce and exceptions for worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether obligatory prayer breaks the immediacy (fawr) in a conditional oath, identify the mufta bihi position, and distinguish between valid and invalid excuses for delay.

Core Idea
Obligatory worship does not break the immediacy of an oath; it is a legal excuse excepted both legally and customarily.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the concept of fawr (immediacy) in conditional divorces. The question: if a wife is commanded to act immediately under threat of divorce, does performing an obligatory prayer constitute a delay that triggers the divorce? The answer hinges on whether prayer is a separate action or a legally recognized excuse. The dispute between Nasr ibn Yahya and Hasan ibn Ziyad illustrates how the Hanafi school weighs custom and obligation in oath-based scenarios.

Guided Path
1
State the common scenario
Identify the basic case: a husband says 'If you do not come to my father's house immediately, you are divorced.' The wife begins preparing but delays by performing activities.
2
Identify the core issue
The dispute centers on whether performing obligatory prayer (ṣalāt) constitutes a break in the immediacy requirement (fawr).
3
State Nasr ibn Yahya's position
Nasr holds that prayer is a separate action (ʿamal ākhar) that cuts off continuity, so the oath is broken.
4
State Hasan ibn Ziyad's position
Hasan holds that obligatory prayer is a legal excuse (ʿudhr sharʿī) and is excepted from the oath both legally and customarily; the oath is not broken.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The text declares 'wa ʿalayhi al-fatwā' (the fatwa is upon this), indicating Hasan's view is operative. The practical consequence: if the wife prays, no divorce occurs.
Distinguish from supererogatory acts and eating/drinking, which do break the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو قال لامرأته إن لم تقومي الساعة وتجيئي إلى دار والدي فأنت طالق ثلاثاً فقامت الساعة ولبست الثياب وخرجت ثم رجعت وجلست حتى خرج الزوج فخرجت هي أيضاً وأتت دار والده بعدما أتاها الزوج لا يحنث لأن رجوع المرأة وجلوسها ما دامت في تهيؤ الخروج لا يكون تركاً للفور، ألا ترى أنه لو أخذها البول فبالت قبل لبس الثياب ثم لبست الثياب لم يحنث ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا قال لامرأته إن لم تجيئي إلى الفراش هذه الساعة فأنت طالق وهما في التشاجر فطال بينهما كان على الفور حتى لو ذهبت إلى الفراش لا يحنث، فإن خافت فوت الصلاة فصلت قال نصر بن يحيى : حنث الرجل لأن الصلاة عمل آخر فينقطع به فور الأول. وعلى قياس الحسن بن زياد لا يحنث وعليه الفتوى ولو اشتغلت بالوضوء للصلاة المكتوبة أو اشتغلت بالصلاة المكتوبة لا يحنث لأنه عذر شرعاً فصار مستثنى من يمينه شرعاً وعرفاً، ولو اشتغلت بالتطوع أو بالوضوء أو أكلت أو شربت حنث لأن هذا ليس بعذر شرعاً .اهـ.
If he said to his wife, 'If you do not get up now and come to my father's house, you are thrice divorced,' and she got up now, put on clothes, went out, then returned and sat until the husband went out, and she also went out and came to his father's house after the husband had come to it, he does not break the oath because the woman's return and sitting as long as she is in preparation for going out is not an abandonment of immediacy. Do you not see that if she was overcome by urine and she urinated before putting on clothes then put on clothes, he does not break the oath. Do you not see that if a man said to his wife, 'If you do not come to bed this hour, you are divorced,' while they were quarreling, and it prolonged between them, it is based on immediacy, so that if she went to bed, he does not break the oath. If she feared missing prayer and thus prayed, Nasr ibn Yahya said: The man breaks the oath because prayer is another action and thus cuts off the immediacy of the first. By analogy, Hasan ibn Ziyad holds that he does not break the oath, and the fatwa is upon this. And if she occupied herself with ablution for obligatory prayer or occupied herself with obligatory prayer, she does not break the oath because it is a legal excuse, thus it becomes excepted from his oath legally and customarily. And if she occupied herself with supererogatory prayer or with ablution, or ate or drank, she breaks the oath because this is not a legal excuse.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the concept of immediate action (fawr) in conditional oaths. The author presents a case where a husband conditions divorce on his wife not going to her father's house immediately. The wife begins preparing but delays. The key issue: does the preparatory phase break the command of immediacy? The author gives examples to illustrate that incidental actions during preparation (like urinating) do not break fawr. The dispute between Nasr ibn Yahya and Hasan ibn Ziyad concerns whether performing obligatory prayer interrupts fawr. Nasr says yes, because prayer is a distinct action. Hasan says no, because it is a religious obligation that excuses the delay. The mufta bihi is Hasan's position. Obligatory prayer and its prerequisite wudu are exceptions; supererogatory acts and eating/drinking are not.

Key Terms
الفور (al-fawr): Immediacy: the requirement that an action be done without delay upon the trigger of a conditional oath.
عذر شرعي (udhr shar'i): A legally recognized excuse that exempts one from performing an action immediately.
حنث (hanith): To break an oath; here it means the husband breaks his oath (divorce takes effect).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in this passage, does a wife break her oath if she performs supererogatory prayer while commanded to go out immediately?
A) Yes, because supererogatory prayer is not a legal excuse.
B) No, because all prayers are valid excuses.
C) Yes, only if she intended to delay.
D) No, because prayer is always an interruption.

Key Takeaway
Obligatory worship is a legal excuse that does not break the immediacy of an oath; the fatwa adopts Hasan ibn Ziyad's view.

The Effect of Quarrel Context on a 'Halal is Haram' Oath
Ruling on an oath using 'halal is haram' in a quarrel, depending on intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the subject of a quarrel determines whether a delayed action breaks a conditional 'halal is haram' oath.

Core Idea
The subject of the quarrel defines the oath's scope: immediate vs. indefinite.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from al-Qunyah regarding the common divorce oath 'halal upon me is haram.' The key is that the context of the quarrel defines what is required. If the quarrel is about a specific action, the oath applies to that action with immediacy; otherwise, a delayed action does not break the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath expression and condition
The husband says 'halal is haram if you do not go out.' Recognize this as a conditional divorce oath.
The condition is 'if you do not go out'.
2
Note the husband's intention
He states he intended immediate exit ('I intended by it immediate going out').
Intention clarifies the required timing.
3
Determine the subject of the quarrel
Check whether the quarrel (al-khusumah) was specifically about her going out or about something else.
The context of the quarrel is the decisive factor.
4
Apply the rule based on the quarrel's subject
If the quarrel was about going out, the oath requires immediate exit; a delayed exit breaks the oath. If the quarrel was about another matter, the delayed exit does not break the oath.
The oath is interpreted narrowly within the quarrel's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : قال لها في الخصومة الحلال علي حرام إن لم تخرجي وقال ما أردت به الخروج للحال ثم خرجت بعد ساعات يحنث إن كانت الخصومة في الخروج وإلا فلا
And in al-Qunyah: He said to her during a quarrel, 'Halal upon me is haram if you do not go out,' and he said, 'I intended by it immediate going out.' Then she went out after hours. He breaks his oath if the quarrel was about going out, otherwise no.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the collection al-Qunyah. The issue: a husband uses the phrase 'halal is haram' (a common oath substitute) as a conditional divorce threat during a quarrel. The condition is 'if you do not go out.' Later, she goes out after hours. The ruling depends on the subject of the quarrel. If the quarrel was specifically about her going out, then the oath is interpreted as requiring immediate exit, so her delayed exit causes the oath to be broken (divorce). If the quarrel was about something else, the oath is not triggered by the exit.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): A quarrel or dispute; the context that clarifies the subject of the oath.
الحلال علي حرام (al-halal 'alayya haram): A common expression used as a divorce oath; it requires fulfillment of the condition or else divorce occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'halal is haram if you do not go out,' when does the husband break his oath?
A) If she goes out after hours, regardless of the quarrel's subject.
B) Only if the quarrel was about her going out.
C) Only if she goes out immediately.
D) If he intended immediate exit, even if quarrel was about something else.

Key Takeaway
The subject of the quarrel determines whether a delayed action breaks a conditional 'halal is haram' oath; immediate compliance is required only when the quarrel concerns that specific action.

Four Divisions of Conditional Divorce and Takhbeer with Tard
Rulings on conditional divorce with strikes and on informing about taking wealth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four possible scopes of a conditional divorce oath based on the husband's intent and external indications, and apply the Hanafi rules for interpreting conditional oaths related to informing about taking wealth.

Core Idea
The interpretation of a conditional divorce oath hinges on the dominant indicator: if the husband intended a specific time or was prevented, the condition is narrowed accordingly; otherwise, it applies permanently.

This passage from al-Jami' al-Saghir presents two rulings that illustrate how the Hanafi school approaches conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq). The first ruling classifies a husband's oath about striking into four scenarios. The second deals with an oath concerning the wife's taking of wealth without informing. Both rulings rely on analyzing intent and the operative phrase to determine the scope of the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two separate oaths
The passage covers two distinct conditional divorce oaths: one based on not striking, and one based on taking wealth without informing. Recognize each case and its unique rules.
2
List the four divisions for the striking oath
For the striking oath, four scenarios are outlined: (1) immediate with external indication, (2) immediate without indication but claimed, (3) forever if intended or no intention, (4) specific limited time not accepted. Note each ruling.
3
Explain the reasoning for each division
External indication (e.g., being prevented) binds the oath to immediacy. Claimed immediacy is believed because it imposes severity. Default is forever. Claim of a specific short time like 'today' is rejected.
4
Analyze the wealth oath ruling
If the wife takes wealth and informs after days, the husband does not break the oath. The phrasing determines immediacy: 'and I did not inform you' (with wa) is immediate; 'if I did not inform you' (without wa) is delayed. Both conditions (taking and not informing) are necessary.
5
Apply the rules to new scenarios
Practice using both sets of rules to determine when a conditional divorce takes effect based on intent, external circumstances, and phrasing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجامع : لو قال لها إن لم أضربك فأنت طالق فهي على أربعة أقسام : فإن كان فيه دلالة الفور بأن قصد ضربها فمنع انصرف إلى الفور، وإن نوى الفور بدون الدلالة يصدق أيضاً لأن فيه تغليظاً، وإن نوى الأبد أو لم تكن له نية انصرف الأبد، وإن نوى اليوم أو الغد لم تقبل نيته . ولو قال لها إن أخذت من مالي شيئاً ولم تخبريني فكذا فأخذت ولم تخبره في الحال ولا قبله وإنما أخبرته بعد أيام لا يحنث إن رأيت سارقاً فلم أخبرك فهو على الفور، وإن قال ولم أخبرك وإن لم أخبرك فعلى التراخي ولا بد من الشرطين. اهـ. ما في القنية.
And in al-Jami': If he said to her, 'If I do not strike you, you are divorced,' then it is of four types: If there is indication of immediacy, such that he intended to strike her but was prevented, it goes to immediacy. If he intended immediacy without any indication, he is believed because it is a severity. If he intended forever or had no intention, it goes to forever. If he intended today or tomorrow, his intention is not accepted. And if he said to her, 'If you take something from my wealth and you do not inform me, then such-and-such,' and she took it and did not inform him instantly nor before, but she informed him after days, he does not break the oath—'if I saw a thief and I did not inform you' it is immediate. And if he said 'and I did not inform you' or 'if I did not inform you,' it is based on delay, and both conditions are necessary.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings from al-Jami'. The first concerns a conditional divorce based on striking: there are four scenarios depending on intent and apparent circumstances. If the husband intended to strike but was prevented, the condition is treated as immediate. If he intended immediacy without clear indication, his claimed intention is accepted because it imposes severity on himself. If he intended forever or had no intention, it applies forever. If he intended a specific day, that is not accepted. The second ruling deals with a conditional divorce triggered by taking wealth without informing. If she informs after days, the oath is not broken because the condition of informing is not immediate; its requirement is fulfilled with any timely informing. However, if the oath is phrased with 'and I did not inform you' (with wa), it is immediate; if with 'if I did not inform you,' it is delayed. Both conditions are needed.

Key Terms
دلالة الفور (dalalat al-fawr): An indication that the oath was intended for immediate action.
التراخي (al-tarakhi): Delay; when the oath is interpreted to apply over time, not immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'If I do not strike you, you are divorced' and he intended the condition to apply forever, how is it interpreted?
A) As immediate, because striking is an immediate act.
B) As forever, because that was his intention.
C) As only for today, because that is the maximum.
D) His intention is not considered.

Key Takeaway
The scope of a conditional divorce oath is determined by intent and external indications; a claim of a limited time is rejected unless evidence supports immediacy.

Intention in Oaths by Allah: Oppressor vs. Oppressed
Rule on who counts in oaths by Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for whose intention is considered in oaths by Allah when the swearer is an oppressor or oppressed, and distinguish between diyanah and qada'.

Core Idea
In oaths by Allah, the oppressed's intention counts; the oppressor's intention is disregarded.

When someone swears by Allah, the question of whose intention matters depends on whether the swearer is acting oppressively or is being oppressed. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi law, as the ruling differs between oaths by Allah and oaths by divorce or manumission. This lesson clarifies the rule for oaths by Allah, along with the difference between religious (diyanatan) and judicial (qada') validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Determine whether the swearer is an oppressor (zalim) or oppressed (mazlum). The passage states that if he is mazlum, his intention applies; if zalim, the intention of the one who made him swear applies.
The classification is based on the context: a zalim uses the oath to unjustly deprive another of a right.
2
Note the scope of the rule
This rule applies only to oaths by Allah, not to oaths of divorce or manumission. The passage highlights that the difference between diyanah and qada' does not appear in oaths by Allah because expiation is a right of Allah.
In divorce/manumission oaths, the rule is different; intention is always considered diyanatan.
3
Understand the authority
The passage attributes this rule to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah). It is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the school.
No intra-Hanafi dispute exists on this specific point.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a hypothetical case, determine which intention is used by the judge and which is accepted religiously.
In court, the judge will accept the intention of the one who made the oath if the swearer is an oppressor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الفرق بين الديانة والقضاء إنما يظهر في الطلاق والعتاق وأما في الحلف بالله تعالى فلا يظهر لأن الكفارة حق الله ليس للعبد فيها حق حتى يرفع الحالف إلى القاضي. وفي الواقعات : إذا استحلف الرجل بالله وهو مظلوم فاليمين على ما نوى وإن كان ظالماً فاليمين على نية من استحلفه وبه أخذ أبو حنيفة ومحمد. وفي اليمين بالطلاق اليمين على نية الحالف وفي الولوالجية من الطلاق: نية تخصيص العام لا تصح . وعند الخصاف تصح حتى إن من حلف وقال كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ثم قال نويت به من بلدة كذا لا تصح نيته في ظاهر المذهب. وقال الخصاف : تصح. وكذا يمنع المكان قوله: (ولا يحنث أصلاً) قال الرملي : أي لو نوى بقوله إن لبست ثوباً جميع ثياب الدنيا لا يحنث أصلاً بلبث ثوب أو ثوبين أو ثلاث أو أكثر لأنه لم يلبس ثياب الدنيا وهو المحلوف عليه تأمل قوله: (وفي اليمين بالطلاق اليمين على نية الحالف ظاهره سواء كان ظالماً أو مظلوماً بدليل ذكره بعد التفصيل في اليمين بالله تعالى فقط ويخالفه عبارة الولوالجية فإنه جعل صحة نيته قول الخصاف إلا أن يقال : المراد أنه على نية الحالف في الديانة لا القضاء فإذا رفع إلى القاضي فلا يصدقه . ثم الظاهر أن كلام الولوالجية خاص بالطلاق لا يشمل اليمين بالله تعالى بدليل سباق الكلام وسياقه، ولما مر من أنه لا مدخل للقضاء في اليمين بالله تعالى لكن يحتاج إلى الفرق بين اليمين بالله تعالى حيث لم تصح فيها النية ديانة إلا إذا كان مظلوماً، وبين اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق حيث صحت ديانة مطلقاً تأمل. ولعل الفرق هتك حرمة اسم الله تعالى واقتطاع حق المسلم بوسيلة اسمه تعالى تأمل. وعبارة قاضيخان هنا رجل حلف رجلاً فحلف ونوى غير ما يريد المستحلف، إن كانت من غصب دراهم إنسان ووقت ما حلفه الخصم عاماً نوى خاصاً لا تصح نيته من ظاهر المذهب. وقال الخصاف تصح لكن هذا في القضاء أما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى نية تخصيص العام صحيحة بالإجماع مذكور في الكتب من مواضع منها الباب الخامس من أيمان الجامع الكبير، وما قاله الخصاف مخلص لمن حلفه ظالم والفتوى على ظاهر المذهب فمتى وقت في يد الظلمة وأخذ بقول الخصاف لا بأس به ا هـ
Then know that the difference between diyanah and qada' only appears in divorce and manumission, but in swearing by Allah Almighty it does not appear because the expiation is a right of Allah, the slave has no right in it such that the swearer would bring it to the judge. And in al-Waqi'at: If a man is made to swear by Allah and he is oppressed, the oath is upon what he intended; but if he is an oppressor, the oath is upon the intention of the one who made him swear, and this is what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad adopted. And in the oath by divorce, the oath is upon the intention of the swearer. And in al-Walwaljiyyah on divorce: Intention to specify the general is not valid. And according to al-Khassaf, it is valid. So much so that if someone swears and says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' then says 'I intended it from such a town,' his intention is not valid in the apparent madhhab. And al-Khassaf said: it is valid. And similarly the prohibition of place: his statement 'and he does not break the oath at all'. Al-Ramli said: meaning if he intends by his statement 'if I wear a garment' all garments of the world, he does not break the oath at all by wearing one or two or three or more because he did not wear the garments of the world, which is the object of the oath. Reflect on his statement: 'And in the oath by divorce, the oath is upon the intention of the swearer' — its apparent meaning is whether he is oppressor or oppressed, as evidenced by his mentioning it after the detail about oaths by Allah only, and the expression of al-Walwaljiyyah contradicts it, for it made the validity of his intention the view of al-Khassaf, unless it is said: what is meant is that it is upon the intention of the swearer in diyanah, not qada'; so if he goes to the judge, he will not believe him. Then it is apparent that the words of al-Walwaljiyyah are specific to divorce and do not include oath by Allah Almighty, as evidenced by the preceding and following context, and because it has passed that qada' has no entry in oath by Allah Almighty. But one needs the difference between oath by Allah Almighty where intention is not valid diyanatan except if he is oppressed, and oath by divorce and manumission where it is valid diyanatan absolutely. Reflect. Perhaps the difference is the violation of the sanctity of the name of Allah Almighty and the cutting off of a Muslim's right by means of His name, reflect. And the expression of Qadikhan here: A man made another swear, and he swore and intended other than what the one who made him swear wanted; if it was about usurping someone's dirhams and at the time the opponent made him swear generally, he intended specific, his intention is not valid in the apparent madhhab. And al-Khassaf said it is valid, but this is in qada'; as for between him and Allah Almighty, intention to specify the general is valid by consensus, mentioned in books from places including the fifth chapter of Ayman of al-Jami' al-Kabir. And what al-Khassaf said is a way out for one who is made to swear by an oppressor, and the fatwa is on the apparent madhhab. So whenever one falls into the hands of oppressors and acts on the view of al-Khassaf, there is no problem.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage establishes a crucial distinction in Hanafi oath law. First, the difference between diyanah and qada' only matters in oaths of divorce/manumission because kaffarah in oaths by Allah is solely between the servant and Allah. Second, in oaths by Allah, if the swearer is oppressed (mazlum), his intention counts; if he is an oppressor (zalim) intending to deprive another of a right, the intention of the one who made him swear (the plaintiff) is considered. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. However, in oaths by divorce, the swearer's intention always counts diyanatan, regardless of being oppressor or oppressed. But there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Khassaf holds that even in court, a general wording can be specified by intention; the apparent madhhab rejects this in court but accepts it diyanatan. The text from al-Walwaljiyyah and Qadikhan shows that specification of a general term by intention is not accepted in court for divorce oaths, but al-Khassaf's view provides a practical relief (makhlas) in cases of oppression. The fatwa is on the apparent madhhab, but acting on al-Khassaf's view when forced is permissible.

Key Terms
ظالم (ẓālim): A person who swears with the intention of depriving another of a right, using the oath unjustly.
مظلوم (maẓlūm): A person who is forced or pressured to swear, and his intention is considered.
ديانة (diyānah): Religious validity; what is binding before Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The oppressor's intention
B) The intention of the one who made him swear
C) No one's intention; the oath is invalid
D) Both intentions are considered equally

Key Takeaway
In oaths by Allah, the oppressed's intention counts; the oppressor's intention is disregarded in court.

Specifying General Words in Divorce Oaths: Al-Khassaf vs. Apparent Madhhab
Intra-Hanafi dispute on specifying general terms in divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between al-Khassaf and the apparent madhhab regarding takhsis al-'amm bi al-niyyah in divorce oaths, identify the mufta bihi, and apply both views to a scenario.

Core Idea
In divorce oaths, intention to specify a general word is valid only diyanatan; in court, the apparent meaning prevails.

When a person swears by divorce using a general expression (e.g., 'every woman I marry is divorced'), can his intention to limit that general to a specific subset be accepted? The Hanafi school is divided: the apparent madhhab (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rejects it in court but accepts it religiously, while al-Khassaf accepts it even in court. This lesson examines the arguments and the practical relief for the oppressed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general wording
The oath uses a general term like 'every woman' (kull imra'ah). The swearer later claims he intended only women of a specific town.
The issue is whether this specification by intention (takhsis al-'amm bi al-niyyah) is valid.
2
State the apparent madhhab position
The apparent madhhab (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that the specification is not valid in court; the general wording applies, so divorcing any woman other than the intended group counts as a breach. However, it is valid diyanatan (religiously).
The passage quotes al-Walwaljiyyah: 'نية تخصيص العام لا تصح'.
3
State al-Khassaf's position
Al-Khassaf holds that the specification is valid even in court. If the swearer intended only women of a particular town, marrying a woman from another town does not trigger divorce.
The passage mentions al-Khassaf's view as a way out (makhlas) for the oppressed.
4
Understand the distinction between diyanah and qada'
The apparent madhhab accepts the specification religiously (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an). Al-Khassaf accepts it in both. The passage clarifies that the general rule 'في اليمين بالطلاق اليمين على نية الحالف' refers to diyanah only.
This distinction is the key to resolving the apparent contradiction in the texts.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its application
The fatwa is on the apparent madhhab, but al-Khassaf's view provides a permissible relief for those forced to swear by oppressors.
The passage concludes: 'والفتوى على ظاهر المذهب ... فمتى وقت في يد الظلمة وأخذ بقول الخصاف لا بأس به'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الفرق بين الديانة والقضاء إنما يظهر في الطلاق والعتاق وأما في الحلف بالله تعالى فلا يظهر لأن الكفارة حق الله ليس للعبد فيها حق حتى يرفع الحالف إلى القاضي. وفي الواقعات : إذا استحلف الرجل بالله وهو مظلوم فاليمين على ما نوى وإن كان ظالماً فاليمين على نية من استحلفه وبه أخذ أبو حنيفة ومحمد. وفي اليمين بالطلاق اليمين على نية الحالف وفي الولوالجية من الطلاق: نية تخصيص العام لا تصح . وعند الخصاف تصح حتى إن من حلف وقال كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ثم قال نويت به من بلدة كذا لا تصح نيته في ظاهر المذهب. وقال الخصاف : تصح. وكذا يمنع المكان قوله: (ولا يحنث أصلاً) قال الرملي : أي لو نوى بقوله إن لبست ثوباً جميع ثياب الدنيا لا يحنث أصلاً بلبث ثوب أو ثوبين أو ثلاث أو أكثر لأنه لم يلبس ثياب الدنيا وهو المحلوف عليه تأمل قوله: (وفي اليمين بالطلاق اليمين على نية الحالف ظاهره سواء كان ظالماً أو مظلوماً بدليل ذكره بعد التفصيل في اليمين بالله تعالى فقط ويخالفه عبارة الولوالجية فإنه جعل صحة نيته قول الخصاف إلا أن يقال : المراد أنه على نية الحالف في الديانة لا القضاء فإذا رفع إلى القاضي فلا يصدقه . ثم الظاهر أن كلام الولوالجية خاص بالطلاق لا يشمل اليمين بالله تعالى بدليل سباق الكلام وسياقه، ولما مر من أنه لا مدخل للقضاء في اليمين بالله تعالى لكن يحتاج إلى الفرق بين اليمين بالله تعالى حيث لم تصح فيها النية ديانة إلا إذا كان مظلوماً، وبين اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق حيث صحت ديانة مطلقاً تأمل. ولعل الفرق هتك حرمة اسم الله تعالى واقتطاع حق المسلم بوسيلة اسمه تعالى تأمل. وعبارة قاضيخان هنا رجل حلف رجلاً فحلف ونوى غير ما يريد المستحلف، إن كانت من غصب دراهم إنسان ووقت ما حلفه الخصم عاماً نوى خاصاً لا تصح نيته من ظاهر المذهب. وقال الخصاف تصح لكن هذا في القضاء أما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى نية تخصيص العام صحيحة بالإجماع مذكور في الكتب من مواضع منها الباب الخامس من أيمان الجامع الكبير، وما قاله الخصاف مخلص لمن حلفه ظالم والفتوى على ظاهر المذهب فمتى وقت في يد الظلمة وأخذ بقول الخصاف لا بأس به ا هـ
Then know that the difference between diyanah and qada' only appears in divorce and manumission, but in swearing by Allah Almighty it does not appear because the expiation is a right of Allah, the slave has no right in it such that the swearer would bring it to the judge. And in al-Waqi'at: If a man is made to swear by Allah and he is oppressed, the oath is upon what he intended; but if he is an oppressor, the oath is upon the intention of the one who made him swear, and this is what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad adopted. And in the oath by divorce, the oath is upon the intention of the swearer. And in al-Walwaljiyyah on divorce: Intention to specify the general is not valid. And according to al-Khassaf, it is valid. So much so that if someone swears and says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' then says 'I intended it from such a town,' his intention is not valid in the apparent madhhab. And al-Khassaf said: it is valid. And similarly the prohibition of place: his statement 'and he does not break the oath at all'. Al-Ramli said: meaning if he intends by his statement 'if I wear a garment' all garments of the world, he does not break the oath at all by wearing one or two or three or more because he did not wear the garments of the world, which is the object of the oath. Reflect on his statement: 'And in the oath by divorce, the oath is upon the intention of the swearer' — its apparent meaning is whether he is oppressor or oppressed, as evidenced by his mentioning it after the detail about oaths by Allah only, and the expression of al-Walwaljiyyah contradicts it, for it made the validity of his intention the view of al-Khassaf, unless it is said: what is meant is that it is upon the intention of the swearer in diyanah, not qada'; so if he goes to the judge, he will not believe him. Then it is apparent that the words of al-Walwaljiyyah are specific to divorce and do not include oath by Allah Almighty, as evidenced by the preceding and following context, and because it has passed that qada' has no entry in oath by Allah Almighty. But one needs the difference between oath by Allah Almighty where intention is not valid diyanatan except if he is oppressed, and oath by divorce and manumission where it is valid diyanatan absolutely. Reflect. Perhaps the difference is the violation of the sanctity of the name of Allah Almighty and the cutting off of a Muslim's right by means of His name, reflect. And the expression of Qadikhan here: A man made another swear, and he swore and intended other than what the one who made him swear wanted; if it was about usurping someone's dirhams and at the time the opponent made him swear generally, he intended specific, his intention is not valid in the apparent madhhab. And al-Khassaf said it is valid, but this is in qada'; as for between him and Allah Almighty, intention to specify the general is valid by consensus, mentioned in books from places including the fifth chapter of Ayman of al-Jami' al-Kabir. And what al-Khassaf said is a way out for one who is made to swear by an oppressor, and the fatwa is on the apparent madhhab. So whenever one falls into the hands of oppressors and acts on the view of al-Khassaf, there is no problem.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage establishes a crucial distinction in Hanafi oath law. First, the difference between diyanah and qada' only matters in oaths of divorce/manumission because kaffarah in oaths by Allah is solely between the servant and Allah. Second, in oaths by Allah, if the swearer is oppressed (mazlum), his intention counts; if he is an oppressor (zalim) intending to deprive another of a right, the intention of the one who made him swear (the plaintiff) is considered. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. However, in oaths by divorce, the swearer's intention always counts diyanatan, regardless of being oppressor or oppressed. But there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Khassaf holds that even in court, a general wording can be specified by intention; the apparent madhhab rejects this in court but accepts it diyanatan. The text from al-Walwaljiyyah and Qadikhan shows that specification of a general term by intention is not accepted in court for divorce oaths, but al-Khassaf's view provides a practical relief (makhlas) in cases of oppression. The fatwa is on the apparent madhhab, but acting on al-Khassaf's view when forced is permissible.

Key Terms
ظالم (ẓālim): A person who swears with the intention of depriving another of a right, using the oath unjustly.
مظلوم (maẓlūm): A person who is forced or pressured to swear, and his intention is considered.
تخصيص العام بالنية (takhṣīṣ al-‘ām bi al-niyyah): Restricting a general word (like 'every woman') to a specific subset by intention (e.g., 'women of that town').
ديانة (diyānah): Religious validity; what is binding before Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent madhhab, if a man swears by divorce 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and intends only women of a certain town, then marries a woman from another town, what is the ruling in court?
A) The divorce does not occur because his intention specifies the general
B) The divorce occurs because the general wording applies
C) The divorce occurs only if the woman is from the intended town
D) The oath is invalid because of the hidden intention

Key Takeaway
In divorce oaths, the court follows the literal wording; specification by intention is accepted only religiously, per the apparent madhhab, while al-Khassaf's view offers relief for the oppressed.
