The Trick to Avoid Two Conditional Oaths
Presents a hilah to avoid breaking two conditional oaths of divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi hilah for avoiding simultaneous violation of two conditional divorce oaths and explain why it is valid.

Core Idea
A lawful sale can cancel the possibility of gifting, thereby avoiding two conflicting oaths.

This passage presents a clever legal stratagem (hilah) from Fath al-Qadir. When a husband and father impose contradictory conditions on the wife's gift of her dowry, she can use a sale to extricate herself. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that lawful means are permissible to avoid sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The husband says: 'If you do not gift me your dowry today, you are divorced.' The father says: 'If she gifts you your dowry, your mother is divorced.' The wife is trapped: gifting triggers the father's oath, not gifting triggers the husband's.
2
Recognize the apparent deadlock
If she gifts, father breaks; if she withholds, husband breaks. She cannot satisfy both oaths directly.
3
Understand the sale transaction
The wife uses the dowry to buy a wrapped cloth from her husband. The dowry becomes payment, so it is no longer in her possession to gift. Both parties agree to a valid sale.
The sale must be genuine and not a sham.
4
See how each condition is avoided
By sunset, the dowry is owned by the husband (as price), so the wife cannot gift it. Thus, the husband's condition (no gift) is not violated. The father's condition (if she gifts) is also not triggered because no gift occurred.
Both oaths remain unbroken.
5
Note the option of inspection
Later, if the wife finds a defect in the cloth, she can exercise khiyar al-ru'yah to return it and reclaim the dowry.
This is a separate legal right and does not affect the validity of the hilah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ليضربنه أو ليكلمنه ومنها لو قال رجل لامرأته إن لم تهبي لي صداقك اليوم فأنت طالق وقال أبوها إن وهبت له صداقك فأمك طالق فحيلة عدم حنثهما أن تشتري منه بمهرها ثوباً ملفوفاً وتقبضه، فإذا مضى اليوم لم يحنث أبوها لأنها لم تهب صداقها ولا الزوج لأنها عجزت عن الهبة عند الغروب لأن الصداق سقط عن الزوج بالبيع، ثم إذا أرادت عود الصداق ردته بخيار الرؤية ؛ الكل في فتح القدير.
And among them: he strikes him or speaks to him, and among them: if a man said to his wife, 'If you do not gift me your dowry today, you are divorced,' and her father said, 'If she gifts you her dowry, your mother is divorced,' then the trick to avoid both breaking (the oath) is that she buys from him with her dowry a wrapped cloth and takes possession of it. Then when the day ends, her father does not break (his oath) because she did not gift her dowry, and neither does the husband because she became incapable of gifting at sunset because the dowry was removed from the husband by the sale. Then if she wants to return the dowry, she returns it by the option of inspection; all of this is in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hilah) from Fath al-Qadir to avoid the simultaneous violation of two conditional divorce oaths. A husband says to his wife: 'If you do not gift me your dowry today, you are divorced.' Her father says: 'If she gifts you your dowry, your mother is divorced.' If she simply gave the dowry to the husband, she would cause her father to break his oath. If she withheld it, she would break her husband's oath. The solution: she uses the dowry to purchase a wrapped cloth from her husband, taking possession of it. By sunset, the husband no longer owns the dowry (it became his property in exchange for the cloth), so the wife cannot gift what she no longer has. Thus, she did not gift the dowry, so the husband's oath is not violated. The father's condition ('if she gifts') is also not fulfilled, as she bought a cloth instead. The father is saved because there was no gift to the husband. Later, the wife may cancel the sale due to a defect (خيار الرؤية) if she finds the cloth unsatisfactory, effectively returning the dowry. This ruling is a practical example of using lawful means to avoid sin while fulfilling the conditions of oaths.

Key Terms
حيلة (hīlah): A lawful stratagem or legal device to achieve a legitimate outcome while technically complying with the wording of an oath or contract.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: the buyer's right to rescind a sale upon seeing a defect in the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hilah given, why does the father not break his oath when the day ends?
A) Because the wife refused to gift the dowry.
B) Because the wife bought a cloth with the dowry, making it no longer in her possession to gift.
C) Because the father's oath becomes void.
D) Because the husband forgot the condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits lawful stratagems to avoid conflicting oaths, such as using a sale to change the legal status of property, thereby avoiding sin while adhering to the letter of the oath.

Knowledge of Impossibility in Conditional Divorce
Ruling on conditional divorce when the person to be met is absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction of knowledge to determine whether a conditional oath is broken when the condition becomes impossible.

Core Idea
Knowledge of impossibility at the oath triggers حنث; ignorance of it does not.

This passage from al-Walwaljiyyah presents a subtle ruling on conditional divorce where the husband swears to enter a city and meet a specific person by night. He enters but finds the person absent and never meets him. The key is his knowledge of the absence when he made the oath. This lesson unpacks the legal logic behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its conditions
The husband says: 'If I do not enter the city tonight and do not meet so-and-so, my wife is divorced.' Two conditions are linked: entering the city AND meeting the person. Failure on either triggers divorce.
The oath uses 'and' (ولم ألق), so both must occur to avoid divorce.
2
Determine what happened
He enters the city but does not find the person at home; he does not meet him until morning. The meeting condition is not fulfilled.
3
Assess his knowledge at oath time
The ruling hinges on whether he knew the person was absent at the moment he swore. If he knew, meeting was impossible from the start. If he did not know, his entry was a genuine attempt.
Knowledge is subjective; the text uses عالماً (knowing).
4
Apply the distinction
If he knew: he breaks his oath (يحنث) because he swore to do an impossible thing, which is not binding, so the condition reduces to 'if I do not meet him' and he fails. If he did not know: he does not break his oath because the impossibility was unforeseen, and his entry was a good faith effort.
This aligns with the Hanafi principle that oaths on impossible matters are void if the impossibility was anticipated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما في الولوالجية من تعليق الطلاق رجل قال إن لم أدخل الليلة البلد ولم ألق فلاناً فامرأته طالق فدخل ولم يصادفه في منزله فلم يلقه حتى أصبح إن كان عالماً بأنه غاب عن المنزل وقت الحلف يحنث، وإن لم يكن عالماً لا يحنث ا هـ.
And among them is what is in al-Walwaljiyyah concerning conditional divorce: a man said, 'If I do not enter the city tonight and do not meet so-and-so, my wife is divorced.' He entered and did not find him in his home, so he did not meet him until morning. If he knew that he (the person) was absent from home at the time of the oath, he breaks his oath. If he did not know, he does not break his oath. End.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This ruling from al-Walwaljiyyah deals with a conditional divorce linked to two actions: entering the city and meeting a specific person. The man swore he would do both tonight; if he fails to meet the person, his wife is divorced. He enters the city but the person is not at home; he does not meet him until morning. The key factor is his knowledge of the person's absence at the time of the oath. If he knew that the person was absent (e.g., he knew he had traveled), then his entering the city does not fulfill the condition because meeting was impossible from the start; thus he breaks the oath. If he was unaware of the absence, his entry was a genuine attempt, and since he could not reasonably meet the person, he does not break the oath. This is based on the principle that an oath to do something impossible is not binding (like the jar case). The knowledge makes the impossibility anticipatory.

Key Terms
يحنث (yahinithu): He breaks his oath, i.e., the condition is violated and the consequence (divorce) takes effect.
عالماً (‘āliman): Knowing at the time of the oath that the person is absent, making fulfillment impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath from al-Walwaljiyyah, under what condition does the husband break his oath?
A) If he knew the person was absent at the time of the oath.
B) If he did not know the person was absent at the time of the oath.
C) If he entered the city before midnight.
D) If the person was present but he didn't see him.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of impossibility at the time of the oath transforms a failed condition into a broken oath; ignorance excuses the breach.
